==PAGE:1==
Master Thesis
Experimental analyses of fl ow and 
turbulence in large porous structures
Kasper Pagh Petersen
January 2016
==PAGE:2==
 
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
In the attempt to contribute to general knowledge of large scale porous structures in oscillating water,
an experimental analysis have been made to determining the ﬂow forces and link these to a PIV
ﬂow visualisation for such structures. The primary application for the results in this thesis is for
equipment in moon pools but also other areas where the application of large scale porous structures
is common, may have interests in the aspects presented in this report.
The porous structure is chosen to consist of generic square cylinders which have been arrange in
side-by-side set up, tandem set up and lastly a large scale porous structure consisting of 2x2 cylinder.
For all set ups the cylinders have been attached on load cells capable of measuring the in-line force
and the lift force when oscillated through the water for KC = 1.1 to 11.3. The channel is relative
narrow making the effect of blockage to be a dominant feature for some of the set ups. Further more
ﬂow visualisation for all set ups have been performed for KC = 10.5. PIV was used to this. The PIV set
up have not been applied with the purpose of highly exact velocity measurements but more as a ﬂow
visualisation with quantitative results as support for explaining force development.
The overall results show that, for the relative simple porous structures, investigated in this project,
the forces and ﬂow development is a direct combination of the two more simple set ups of side-
by-side set up and tandem set up. If the force coefﬁcients are normalised with the ones of a single
cylinder, the product of the two simple set ups yields close to the same values as found for the 2x2
set up. This is supported by the ﬂow characteristics which shows the same. The shielding effect from
the cylinders is the most dominating effect in the porous structure when the gap-ratios are the same
all over. This may not be expected for more complex structures.
The governing aspects for a large scaled porous structure is the potential effect of blockage, the jet-
ﬂow pattern inside the structure and the shielding effect which the elements may experience from
each other. All of which are dependent on the size of the size and gap widths of the structure.

Preface
This Maser Thesis is the ﬁnal part of the Master in Civil Engineer at the Technical University of Den-
mark. The title of the thesis is ’Experimental analyses of ﬂow and turbulence in large porous struc-
tures’. The thesis have been written by appointment to the Department of Mechanical Engineering
at DTU with the equivalent work of 30 ECTS points. The project had a duration of 5 month, running
from the 3. of August 2015 to the 3. of January 2016.
I would like to thank to my primary supervisor Professor Erik Damgaard Christensen at DTU Mek.,
who have guided and supported me thought the project with great professionalism and optimism
even though the project had quite some trouble for a long time.
Also a thank you should also be directed to my supervisor Stefan Carstensen which have been of
invaluable help with the experimental set up. Both when things have worked as they should and
especially during long periods of break down where the help trouble shooting and repair of any kind
have been the key to making the project to succeed.
Lastly a great thank you should go to Claus Grølsted, Poul-Erik Hyldbo and Thomas Hornbeck Hald
at the DTU Mek. work shop, from whom I have received a great amount of help and production
expertise in order to make my experimental set up work at the best possible level.

1
Introduction
The offshore and marine related industries are capable of producing more advanced equipment and
structures than ever, which increase the demand of detailed knowledge about the performance. This
naturally requires a higher level of knowledge to the physical processes which affects the equipment
and structures. A ﬁeld where the details still are in the process of enlightenment, is for large porous
structures subjected to oscillating ﬂows, which is the focus of this thesis. A Large porous structure
can be many things including armour- and ﬁlter layers in rubble mound breakwaters, cover- and
protection layers for scour protection and equipment for the offshore industry, especially for moon-
pool applications where the forces on the structure lowered into the moon pool is heavily inﬂuencing
the damping of the water. See Figure 1.1 below for examples.
(a) The large armour rocks in
break waters makes up a porous
structure
(b) Subsea tree is a complex large
porous structure
Figure 1.1: Examples of large porous structures
Within the latest years, studies concerning turbulence in small sized porous media consisting of
grains and particles have emerged (see e.g. Patil and Liburdy (2013)), showing a growing interest
in the ﬁeld. In the area of large scale porous structures, the topic of sediment transport and force dis-
tributions within rubble mound break waters have been of interest. Also studies of square cylinders
subjected to oscillating water have caught the interest in the past years (See e.g. Chern et al. (2010) ).
Many of the studies have been numerical, thus the experimental data covering ﬂows in large porous
structures are very limited. Also the complexity in the structure, which have been studied previously,
have been relatively small where the only few structural components have been used.
The ongoing topics for the large porous structures, are the inﬂuence of the spacing between the sub-
jects deﬁning the porosity of the structure, the blocking ratio for a structure in a conﬁned ﬂow envir-
1
==PAGE:11==
2
onment and the effect of KC number and Reynolds. Also the alignment of the objects in the porous
structure is of interest.
This thesis have the purpose of experimental investigation of large scaled porous structures, as the
work title indicates. This leaves a great amount of freedom to chose the geometry of the structure.
It was chosen to use another master theses as inspiration for the porous structure. About half a year
before this thesis was initiated, Ann-Soﬁe Bjorke (Bjø rke, 2015) made a numerical study of hydro-
dynamic forces on complex structures with application in moon pools. The geometrics analysed in
the thesis of Bjø rke (2015) are essentially large scale porous structures, as the individual structural
members are modelled as square cylinders with different spacing and size. The reason for choosing
the numerical model set up as direct inspiration to the experimental set up was the possibility to test
some of the results obtained in the numerical model against the experimental results. It is not inten-
ded to be a side by side compare as the numerical work only serves as a reference, why the compares
between numeric and experimental results are relative short.
The experimental set up consist of a channel only 280 mm wide which simulates the conﬁned space
of a moon pool which was used in the thesis of Bjø rke (2015). The time frame allowed only for the
most basic structures consisting of a side-by-side (1x2) arrangement, a tandem (2x1) arrangement
and the combined case of four cylinders placed in a square conﬁguration (2x2) which serves as the
only real porous structure.
The cylinders are attached on load cells which register the loads as they are oscillated for KC-numbers
varying from 1.1 to 11.3. The gap-ratio between the cylinders will very between 0.5,1 and 2 times the
cylinder diameter in order to investigate the effect of interaction. The results from the force meas-
urements are presented in Section 5.
Along with the force measurements ﬂow visualisation for KC = 10 is made to be able to link the meas-
ured forces to the ﬂow pattern. The overall goal have been ﬂow visualisation by use of PIV (Particle
Image Visualisation). PIV makes it possible to have quantitatively information about the ﬂow velo-
cities. The results from the ﬂow visualisation are presented in Section 6.
The hope is to contribute with knowledge within the area of large scaled porous structures. The ap-
plication of the knowledge is intended to be of interest in areas of all kind where large scale porous
structures are present but due to the direct link to the study of Bjø rke (2015) the most obvious con-
trition is for structural porous structures placed narrow channels or moon pools.
==PAGE:12==
2
Theory and methods
A study of experimental kind requires both theoretical knowledge and technical know-how in order
to obtain valid results. This Chapter summarises relatively brieﬂy the theory and methods used in
this project.
2.1
Forces and ﬂow around cylinders
The circular cylinder is the most common and most analysed shape among cylinders used in marine
environment with application in off shore platforms, windmill foundations pipelines among others.
There for this section will take the circular cylinder as a starting point and when describe in what
places a square cylinder deviates from this.
2.1.1
Steady ﬂow around a cylinder
A body submerged in water will, in addition to the buoyancy, experience ﬂow forces if the water
is in motion relative to the body. This force depends on the relative velocity, U, the size and the
geometry of the body. The force will is proportional to the velocity, but how the proportions forms
relative to geometry will vary depending on the shape because of the ﬂow characteristics changes
with the velocity. See Section 2.1.2 for a short summery of forces on cylinders. In order to link the
ﬂow characteristics to an non-dimensional relation for the velocity, regardless of the size of the body
and type of ﬂuid, the Reynolds number is introduced.
Re = DU
ν
(2.1)
where D the diameter of the cylinder, U is the ﬂow velocity and ν is the dynamic viscosity. For water
at 20◦C the dynamic viscosity is close to ν = 1.0·10−6. As Reynolds number increase from zero, the
ﬂow around a circular cylinder will start as a creeping ﬂow, where no separation of the streamlines
will take place. As Reynolds number is increased further, the ﬂow will begin the separate and form a
laminar vortex street in the wake of the cylinder, which will become turbulent as Re increases. See
Sumer and Fredsø e (2006) for more detailed description of the ﬂow regimes for a circular cylinder.
The reason for the development of vortices can be found in the boundary layer between the cylinder
and the water. On the surface of the cylinder, the water is not moving - a no-slip condition. Close
to the wall, in the shear layer, the velocity of the water increases from zero at the cylinder surface
to the free stream velocity. This velocity gradient, in conjunction with the internal friction of the
water, creates a rotation (vorticity) in the boundary layer which then causes the vortices to form. The
point where the vortex is released from the boundary layer and into the wake is called the separation
point. The vortices form at both sides of the cylinder, but with opposite vorticity. This makes the
vortices to drag each other across the wake and release each other in turn at a given frequency. The
3
==PAGE:13==
2.1. FORCES AND FLOW AROUND CYLINDERS
4
shedding frequency chances with Reynolds number. If the vortex shedding frequency is denoted fv
a non-dimensional expression for the shedding frequency reads
St = fvD
U
(2.2)
This is called the Strouhal number. As the velocity increase, so does the shedding frequency as vor-
tices builds up faster at higher ﬂow velocities. Speciﬁc for a circular cylinder the Strouhal number
reaches a point where it becomes approximate constant, but only up to Reynolds numbers around
Re = 3·105 where the separation points goes from being laminar to turbulent. This makes the separ-
ation points move down stream on the cylinder and closer together, allowing the shedding to occur
more frequently. This is the transition from subcritical to supercritical. A discontinuity happens
again when transition from supercritical to transcritical happens. See Sumer and Fredsø e (2006)
for more details. The roughness of the surface does also have an impact on the shedding frequency,
since a more rough surface introduces turbulence at lower velocities than a smooth surface. For the
circular cylinder this implies that the separation becomes turbulent for lover Re.
In contrast to a circular cylinder, which have the separation points determined by the ﬂow regime, a
square cylinder with sharp edges will always have the separation points at the corners of the cylinder.
The sharp edge will cut the boundary layer off and force it to release the vorticity into the wake. Even
at low Re separation will happen, which is also why a creeping ﬂow around an object with sharp
edges may not take place. For the same reason it is not expected to see discontinuities in vortex
shedding frequencies for a square cylinder, but much more constant Strohaul number values.
2.1.2
Forces on cylinders in steady ﬂow
The cylinder will be subject to forces from a pressure forming around the cylinder as well as the fric-
tion between the water and surface but the most dominant force is the pressure force. The force on a
cylinder is divided into in-line force (force in ﬂow direction) and the transverse force (force perpen-
dicular to ﬂow direction). These are also refereed to as drag- and lift force, respectively in a steady
ﬂow environment. The friction will contribute to the drag force and the pressure will contribute to
both the drag force and lift force. The pressure around the cylinder is changing in time due to vortex
shedding. The mechanism of vortex shedding creates a resulting force changing from side to side.
This makes the drag force oscillate around a mean value and the lift force around zero.
The drag force is the sum of the mean drag force and a ﬂuctuating part, oscillating with a frequency
twice the vortex shedding frequency.
FD = F D +F ′
D
(2.3)
The mean drag force is related to the free stream velocity by the mean drag coefﬁcient C D as
F D = 1
2ρDC DU 2
(2.4)
where ρ is the density of the water. The ﬂuctuating drag force is deﬁned on similar form, but the drag
coefﬁcient is now the ﬂuctuating C ′
D.
F ′
D = 1
2ρDC ′
DU 2
(2.5)
The drag force on a circular cylinder undergoes changes as the vortex shedding changes character-
istic. At the transition from subcritical to supercritical, a drag crisis happens, as the septation points
move closer together, creating a more narrow wake. In the wake, the ﬂow is weaker than the outer
ﬂow which results in a negative pressure on the cylinder. A more narrow wake will generate a smaller
==PAGE:14==
5
CHAPTER 2. THEORY AND METHODS
area for the negative pressure to act, thus a smaller drag force at the drag crisis.
The square cylinder will have separation points at the corners and not have them to move around.
There for, the varying drag coefﬁcient relative to Re should not take place as such. Changes in the
wake due to changes in turbulence production and other factors may introduce small change to the
drag coefﬁcient in relation to Re. However, it seems to be general accepted to assume the the drag
coefﬁcient is uninﬂuenced by Re as the separation points does not move. This assumption is used
for this study as well.
The lift force is like wise deﬁned as a mean and a ﬂuctuating part.
FL = F L +F ′
L
(2.6)
The the mean lift, F is zero, for a cylinder uninﬂuenced by walls and incoming turbulence, as the
vortices will shed from both sides with approximate equal magnitude. This is also the case for a
square cylinder, but if placed in an angle opposing to the stream lines in the ﬂow, the lift force will
have a mean component. The ﬂuctuating component can be written in the same form as for the drag
force
F ′
L = 1
2ρDC ′
LU 2
(2.7)
The lift force oscillates with the same frequency as the vortex shedding.
2.1.3
Oscillating ﬂow around cylinders
In oscillating ﬂows, an important parameter for describing the ﬂow around objects, is the Keulegan-
Carpenter number, or just KC-number. The KC number deﬁnes the horizontal stroke length of the
water particles relative to the size of the object as
KC = UmTw
D
(2.8)
where Um is the maximum horizontal velocity in the period, Tw is the period of the motion.
The fact that the ﬂow reverses every so often, makes a difference in how the vortex formation and
shedding takes place. For low KC numbers the vortex does not shed as for steady currents, since the
vortex do not have enough time to develop, before the ﬂow reverses. For higher KC numbers, the
ﬂow will induce vortex shedding either in a steady current fashion where the vortex is shed from the
separation point or by the vortex being washed over the cylinder by the return ﬂow which, then frees
the vortex. The number of vortices being shed for every period, have given names to the different
KC-ranges where they occur. Flows with KC-numbers where only one pair of vortices are shed, is
called the single pair regimes. The range where two pairs are shed is called the Double pair regimes
and so on.
A parameter often used in the literature, when dealing with oscillatory ﬂow around cylinders, is the
β-value deﬁned as
β = Re
KC
⇔
β = D2
νT
(2.9)
A given β-value links the Reynold number for a given cylinder to the KC number, as Equation (2.9)
suggests. This makes the β-value of big importance for cylinders with circular cross section geometry,
but for cylinder with sharp edges, the effect of varying Re (or β) should not be affecting the drag
forces, as also described in Section 2.1.2.
==PAGE:15==
2.1. FORCES AND FLOW AROUND CYLINDERS
6
2.1.4
Forces on cylinder in oscillating ﬂow
The forces in oscillatory ﬂow is also a different story. The drag force will oscillates around a mean
drag force, which now is zero, if only waves are present. The velocity changes direction every half
period, which requires a rewrite of Equation (2.4) to take the direction of ﬂow into account. The force
per unit length reads
FD = 1
2ρDCDU|U|
(2.10)
Besides the pressure from the ﬂow velocity, the hydrodynamic mass force also inﬂuence the object.
This force is the effect of the acceleration of water, acting on the object. According to Newtons 2nd
law, this creates a resulting force which is called the hydrodynamic mass force. Per unit length, this
is expressed as
Fm = ρCm A ˙U
(2.11)
where Cm is the hydrodynamic mass coefﬁcient, A is the cross-sectional area of the object and ˙U
is the acceleration of the water. The hydrodynamic mass coefﬁcient depends on the shape of the
object, as different shapes will be affected differently by the water.
The last force contribution for an object in wave generated oscillatory ﬂow, is the Froude-Krylov
force. This force takes into account the pressure gradient from the outer ﬂow region, which drives
the ﬂow in an oscillatory manner. This force per length is simply as follows
FFK = ρA ˙U
(2.12)
All these force contributions sum up to the Morrison Equation.
F = 1
2ρDCDU|U|+ρCM A ˙U
(2.13)
where CM = (Cm +1) collecting the Hydrodynamic mass force and Froude-Krylov force in one term.
For the case where the cylinder is moved rather than the water, the Froude-Krylov force is not present.
In stead the mass of the cylinder have to be taken into account as well. If the density of the cylinder
equals the density of the surrounding water, the contribution of the cylinder will correspond to the
Froud-krylov force. This will be elaborated upon in Section 2.1.5.
The lift force is still present, but as the vortex shedding is causing the lift, the KC number have inﬂu-
ence on the lift force patten. For very low KC numbers, where no vortices are formed, no lift is formed.
Flows with KC numbers, where one pair of vortices are formed, a lift force will be generated due to the
pressent of vortex development. If the KC-number allows for the vortices to be shed, the vortices will
bd washes over the cylinder as the ﬂow reverses. This create an additional lift force. This generates
lift forces with higher frequency than for the steady current as an extra lift force is experienced by the
wash-over of the vortices.
==PAGE:16==
7
CHAPTER 2. THEORY AND METHODS
2.1.5
Determine CD and CM from the experiment
For the case of this experiment, the cylinder is moving in an oscillatory manner, in order to simulate
the effect of an oscillatory ﬂow. This introduces a small change to the Morrison Equation presented
in Equation (2.13) as the mass of the cylinder is to be taken into account. For the case where not all
the cylinder is submerged in the water, Equation (2.13) is rewritten as the total force acting on the
cylinder
Ftotal = 1
2ρDCDU|U|H +ρCm A ˙U H +m ˙U
(2.14)
where m is the mass of the cylinder being accelerated and H is the length of the cylinder submerged
in water.
The total measured force from the experiment does not by it self reveal the drag and inertia coefﬁ-
cients. A method to evaluate the force measurement, described by Sumer and Fredsø e (2006), is the
Least Square Method. This is based on the idea that the coefﬁcients are determined so that the mean
squared difference, between the predicted and the measured force, is as small as possible.
The predicted force is deﬁned from the Morrison equation as
Fp(t) = fdU(t)|U(t)|+ fi ˙U(t)
(2.15)
where the drag related and inertia related terms are respectively

(2.16)

2.1.6
The effect of blockage
The term blockage is used for a conﬁned ﬂow, where a subject sufﬁcient big relative to the ﬂow chan-
nel width is disturbing the possible cross sectional area to an extent, that the velocity increase around
the subject. This makes it difﬁcult to use the measured or predicted free stream ﬂow velocity to cal-
culate the force on the subject as the subject increases the ﬂow velocity with an unknown magnitude.
There for it is common practice to increase the drag coefﬁcient instead in order to compensate for
the local increased velocity.
==PAGE:17==
2.2. FLOW THROUGH LARGE POROUS STRUCTURES
8
The blockage ratio is deﬁned as γ = D/b i.e. the diameter of the cylinder or object divided by the
width of the channel (See Figure 3.3).
Blockage in steady current for a single square cylinder have been studied numerically and exper-
imental by Davis et al. (1984). This study have experimentally how the increase of blockage ratio
results in higher drag. It have also shown the development of the wake for different velocity proﬁles
and ﬁnds that the effect of blockage is not only a question of the geometry, but also the how the ﬂow
develops in front and behind the object.
The number of studies of blockage in oscillating water is limited and almost non-existing when
searching only experimental studies. A numerical study which should be mentioned is Anagnosto-
poulos and Minear (2004) who found that blockage for a single ﬁxed circular cylinder is not existing
for blockage below 0.2. It was found that the drag increase with the blockage ratio, just for the steady
ﬂow. On the other hand the contribution of the hydrodynamic mass force was found to decrease for
increasing blockage ratios.
An experimental study from Okajima et al. (1997) for a quadratic oscillating cylinder shows that the
blockage is not always causing the drag coefﬁcients to increase. It is shown that the blockage effect
can create a decrease in the drag coefﬁcient for low blockage ratios ( O(0-0.1)) before an increase is
experienced.
2.2
Flow through large porous structures
The ﬂow through a large structure can be complex to describe i general as it depends much upon the
geometry. Few studies have been made in order to describe the mechanisms of this in general, but
many studies have been done with more speciﬁc structures in focus.
To begin with, the numerical study of Chern et al. (2010) can be mentioned, which treats the interac-
tion of oscillatory ﬂow with a pair of side-by-side square cylinders. It describes a jet-like ﬂow through
in the gap between the cylinders, which have impact on the development of vortices between the
cylinders as 7 > KC < 10 results in symmetric vortex formation and for KC > 10 the vortex formation
becomes more and more chaotic. The interaction effect is present for gap distances of 0.5D and 1D
but for the last case of 2D the interaction was not apparent.
A study which relates to this project is by Anagnostopoulos and Dikarou (2011), where a numerical
study of four circular cylinders in a square set-up was investigated. It shows pattern where for in-
creasing KC numbers, the ﬂow becomes more chaotic. The vortices form symmetrical around the
center line of the total set-up, but not symmetrical around the individual cylinder, making the lift
force give a net-lift force in opposite directions for the lower and upper row of cylinders. This study
is for circular cylinder which means that direct compare to this project is difﬁcult.
2.3
Particle Image Velocimetry
Particle image velocimetry, or just PIV, is a method of ﬂow visualisation which can be used to determ-
ine the instant velocity ﬁeld of a ﬂow. A brief overview of the technology are explained in this section
and the equipment and special aspects used for this investigation, is described in Section 3.3. See
e.g. Tropea et al. (2007) for a more detailed walk-through of the PIV theory.
Under normal circumstances, water does not reveal the ﬂow characteristics visually, as the liquid is
transparent. By adding small tracer particles to the water, the motion becomes visible and record-
able, when illuminated by a laser sheet. A principal sketch can be seen in Figure 2.1.
==PAGE:18==
9
CHAPTER 2. THEORY AND METHODS
Figure 2.1: Principal behind PIV. From http://aim2.dlr.de/typo3temp/pics/cbc490e853.jpg
This makes it possible to study the motion of the ﬂow in a limited section of the water with a laser
sheet. A digital camera records two or more images over a short interval, ∆t, from which the particle
displacement ∆x and ∆y can determined for one parts of the image at a time. If ∆t →0, the in-
stantaneous velocity can be determined from which the velocity gradient ﬁeld ∂u/∂x (using Einstein
notation) can be determined for the recorded area.
Both 3D and 2D PIV is possible, but for this project the 2D PIV will be sufﬁcient, as the primary mo-
tion of the ﬂow will be in one plane only.
Several aspects have to be thought of, in order to produce usable recordings. First of all, the laser has
to be able to illuminate the area interest. This implies either that the objects placed in the water have
to be transparent in order to avoid shadow areas or two or more lasers are used in order to illuminate
shadow areas.
The light intensity have to be sufﬁcient for the camera to record the particles imaged as dots rather
than traces. A mathematical meaning of this is, that the illumination time (or exposure time depend-
ing on the equipment) δt should fulﬁl Equation (2.21). Note that δt is not the same as ∆t which is
the time between two recordings.
δt << dim.
vM
(2.21)
where dim. is the diameter of the particle image, v is the velocity and M is the optical magniﬁcation.
This set the requirement for the light intensity of the laser, since a short light pule got to have higher
energy density than a long, for the camera to detect the reﬂected light of the particles. In practice it
can be difﬁcult to fulﬁl this requirement exactly, but a visual inspection of the images will revile if the
exposure time is sufﬁcient.
The speed required for many applications of PIV, exceeds the capability of most digital cameras. By
synchronise the laser with the camera shutter so the shutter opens, the laser ﬁres before the shutter
closes again, the laser pule determines the speed of capturing the particles.
The focus plane have to be illumed by the laser sheet in order for the camera to record sharp, well
deﬁned images of the particles. It may not be possible to avoid that some particles outside of the
focus plane will get captured by the camera, but it is important to minimise this. The use of high-
and low pass ﬁlters can clean out some disturbances, but if the image of the illuminated particles are
==PAGE:19==
2.3. PARTICLE IMAGE VELOCIMETRY
10
not good in the ﬁrst place, it is hard to extract the information that is needed. A relative thin laser
sheet will minimise this.
When the images have been captured, it is necessary to evaluate them by correlation analysis.
2.3.1
Correlation analysis
The method of analysing the recorded data can be done in two ways, since different density of particle
images requires different evaluation techniques. In low-image-density PIV it is possible to determ-
ine the individual particle path from image to image, if the distance between the particles are bigger
than the distance they travel. Typically this is not the case, because it is difﬁcult to operate with travel
distances so small.
Instead a high-image-density PIV method can be used, where the particle position from image to
image is determined from spatial correlation analysis where the most likely path is predicted for each
particle.
Imagine a set of two images where the particles have moved in between the exposure of the images.
These images are divided into small areas called interrogation areas. The size of these areas have
to be big enough to hold several particles in order to produce reliable results. By detecting each
particle in both of the images, it is possible to make a correlation between the movement with in the
interrogation areas. All the particles in the area are linked to each other on turn and the displacement
and direction is registered. By correlating the interrogation areas with each other a correlation peak
will show the most likely displacement of all the particles in the interrogation area. This is done for
all of the interrogation areas and the full velocity map can be generated. Mathematically this can be
written as
R(s) =
Z
A
W1(x)I1(x)W2(x+s)I2(x+s)
(2.22)
where R(s) is the spatial cross correlation of the interrogation areas, W1 and W2 is the window func-
tions for the interrogation areas and I1 and I2 is the images. The velocity with the highest correlation
is the one which are most likely to be the real velocity. A more detailed description and further refer-
ences can be seen in Tropea et al. (2007).
In order for the correlation algorithms to produce reliable PIV results the captured particle density
should be high enough to produced unambiguous results. If for some reason the particles move
out of the laser sheet or into a shaddow area, loss of correlation can happen and a false velocity
prediction may be made. Also if the particle movement is too big relative to the interrogation area, the
correlation can decrease. As a rule of thumb a particle displacement of 1/4 of the interrogation area
should yield a good balance between the particle displacement and interrogation area size (Tropea
et al. (2007) ). For an oscillatory ﬂow this may be difﬁcult as the velocity gradient is present. This is a
major drawback of PIV.

3
Experimental set-up
This section will describe in detail how the experiment is set up in the available facilities at DTU.
As always, when doing experimental work, challenges with the equipment have been encountered.
All the technical aspects of getting the experiment to work as intended, including the problems and
issues will also be described to clarify and document the work behind the set-up.
3.1
Channel, Cylinders and Experiment Set-Up
As described in the introduction, the geometries chosen for the experimental set-up comes from the
numerical investigation done by Bjø rke (2015). The dimensions used in the numerical study are full
scale and cannot be tested in the facilitates at DTU. It may not be feasible either to use a facility which
can replicate the set-up in full scale. The geometries in this experiments is scaled 1 : 25 compared to
Bjø rke (2015).
The geometry of the facility used, is sketched in Figure 3.1 including the 2x2 experiment set-up, in
order to get a feel of the size. The ﬂume used is a bit over sized but this is no problem as such.
Figure 3.1: Deﬁnition sketch of channel. The white area is glass and the hatched area is made of gray plastic.
A plate have been installed to crease a 280 mm channel for the experiment set up. See Figure 3.3 for close up
on the experiment set-up
A 2.5 meter long plate is placed in the channel to be able to narrow down the width to 280 mm.
The channel deﬁned by the side of the ﬂume and the plate is not closed at the ends. This is manly
due to the fact that the moving set-up is acting as a wave generator. If the ends were closed, it would
reﬂect the wave energy and amplify the generated waves, leading to undesired interference. Since the
cylinders that are moving rather than the water, the length of the plate is not as critical as if it have
11
==PAGE:21==
3.1. CHANNEL, CYLINDERS AND EXPERIMENT SET-UP
12
been the other way around. If the water had been moving instead, the plate should have sufﬁcient
length in order to avoid the disturbances from boundary effects at the narrowed channel entries.
Section 3.1.1 takes a closer look at the geometry of the set-up in the narrowed channel and describes
the different combinations of cylinder constellations.
To move the set up the ﬂume have a carriage mounted on rails which is used. This is oscillated by a
hydraulic piston as seen in Figure 3.2. A laser sheet shoot through the glass wall of the channel for
the PIV.
Figure 3.2: Sketch of the actual set up seen from above. The carriage supports on the rails mounted on the side
of the ﬂume.
In the following sections, the different aspects of this set-up is described in detail.

A validation experiment is performed in order to test the ability to predict the forces on a single
square cylinder. This serves as a veriﬁcation of the set-up and proof of concept for the visualisation
with PIV.
The constellation with 2x1 i.e. two cylinder after each other is to investigate shielding effect of the cyl-
inders. In oscillatory ﬂow, the two cylinders will change the role of being the shield and the shielded
with the ﬂow reversal.
This constellation with 1x2 i.e. two cylinders side by side serves the purpose to investigate the effect
of blocking and how the ﬂow develops through the cylinders. It may be expected to see the ﬂow
velocity increase between the two cylinders.
The last constellation with 2x2 serves the purpose of connecting the observations found in the pre-
vious constellations and see the effect of a porous structure.
3.1.2
Moving the set-up
A carriage, mounted on rails, forms the basis of mounting the cylinders. In order to move the carriage,
a hydraulic double ended cylinder piston is used as seen on Figure 3.2. To control the movement, a
computer is set up to produce a sinusoidal output signal which is ampliﬁed and sent into a control
box which controls a servo valve on top of the piston cylinder. A given ampere signal to the servo
valve, results in an extension or reduction of the piston relative to neutral position. On the side of the
cylinder a LVDT is attached, which is used for feedback to the control box. It lets the control box to
correct for any false displacement of the piston caused by external forces. This LVDT cannot be used
for the displacement measurements, why an external string recorder have been applied for that.
The hydraulic pressure is generated by a compressor with a working pressure of 100 Bar. This powers
the piston with a sufﬁcient amount of force. The periods used for the experiment will be of order
T = O(1)s and higher. Lower periods will create such big forces and velocities in the set-up, that
safety and the equipment may be in danger.
The computer is set to use an output frequency of 500Hz. If the signals output frequency gets too
low, it introduces vibrations from the piston, as the movement begins to happen in small jumps. It
is important that the hydraulic piston moves as smooth as possible as even slight vibration will be
picked up in the force measurements.
Unfortunately the state of the available control box is a bit questionable in terms of smooth running.
No problems are observed in the consistency of the displacement magnitude and timing of the peri-
ods, but an un-smooth movement is observed during runs. The hydraulics was send to service in
==PAGE:23==
3.2. LOAD CELLS
14
hope that it would ﬁx this problem. It turned out, that it was the control of the piston which causes
trouble. As the piston extends or subtracts and reaches its desired max displacement and starts to
go the other way, it makes a little jump or impulse. This triggers a spike in any force measurement
of the cylinders. The issue was not solved during the project but a lot of time was spend as seen in,
Appendix I. Fortunately it is possible to ﬁlter the effect of the un-smooth movement away as seen in
Section 4.2
Alignment issues
The forces to be measured in this series of experiments are relative small due to the small cylinders
(see the next section about the cylinders). This causes some problems which becomes clear in Sec-
tion 5. The main issue is that the set up is very difﬁcult, if not impossible, to align perfectly so the
cylinder move parallel to each other and the ﬂow directing every time. The carriage is rather over
sized for the scale of the experiment which means that the overall precision is compromised by the
carriage, as it can move a bit from side to side during runs. It may only be half a centimetre, but as the
cylinders are only four centimetres in diameter it is a relative big displacement. This can make the
cylinders to move non-parallel to the desired path, making the otherwise careful initial alignment
of cylinders on the carriage, somewhat useless. Especially the lift forces have suffered from this as
seen in Section 5. The miss alignment have not compromised the overall validity of the in-line forces
which are relative good.
3.1.3
Cylinders
To create the different conﬁgurations of structural members a total of four square cylinders have
been produced as the one shown in Figure 3.4.
Figure 3.4: Deﬁnition sketch of cylinder with two load cells attached. The alu-rpoﬁle is 40 × 40mm and the
load cells are attached with M6 bolts
The aluminium part is made of an 40×40 mm sharp edged aluminium proﬁle with material thickness
of 2.5 mm. It is important that the cylinders have sharp edges, as it makes separation of the vortices
take place at the same point every time regardless of the Reynolds number. A box of transparent
polycarbonate is glued to the bottom. The polycarbonate makes it possible to shoot the laser sheet
through the cylinder to illuminate the particles for the PIV all over the channel width. The mass of
the cylinders are roughly 390 grams.
3.2
Load Cells
The load cells, which are used for this experiment, is a TAL201 from HT-censors with a maximum
capacity of 10kg load at the tip of the load cell, See Figure 3.5.
==PAGE:24==
15
CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL SET-UP
Figure 3.5: TAL201 from HT-Sensors
As a cylinder is attached to the load cell, the arm at which the load is applied to the load cell, enhance
quite a bit compared to the intended use. The loads which is expected to be measured are well below
the 10kg capacity of the load cells, but the long arm creates bigger strain on the load cells. To be sure
that the load cell material will not start to yield or snap, this load cell have been chosen over a smaller
load cell. Further more, a bigger load cell gives a more stiff system, pushing the eigenfrequency to
higher frequencies than a smaller load cell would have done. The frequencies for the movement
of the carriage and set-up is no bigger than 1Hz corresponding to a period of T=1s. Much higher
frequency for the carriage is not possible for a safe execution of the experiments.
The draw back is that the signal from the load cell can be a bit vague relative to the noise for small
loads.
The forces intended to be measured are the lift force and the in-line force. The load cells are mounted
together by a special mount which allows two load cells to measure loads in directions perpendicular
to each other as the picture in Figure 3.6 shows.
Figure 3.6: Mount for two load cells to measure both lift and in-line forces. In the back ground is a preliminary
ampliﬁer for the load cell signals. The cylinders is to the left in the picture.
This set-up enables the possibility of measuring lift and in-line forces at the same time. The load
cells placed as the top one is ﬁxed to an aluminium proﬁle which is attached to the carriage with
clamps. A total of eight load cells have been applied at once. For this purpose a multi channel load
==PAGE:25==
3.3. PIV-SYSTEM
16
cell ampliﬁer have been produced by the Instrument group at the DTU Mechanics Department. This
is design to have ampliﬁcation of 1000 times for the signal of the load cell output.
It is worth mentioning that since the load cells measure the section force and not the bending mo-
ment, the uneven mass distribution of the cylinders does not effect the force measurements.
3.3
PIV-system
The purpose of using PIV in this project is not to produce highly accurate velocity measurement.
As the set up moves relative to the camera, this is simply not possible as described in Section 4.1.3.
Instead, the purpose of PIV is to produce measurements accurate enough to be able to visualise the
ﬂow patterns in and around the porous structure consisting of square cylinders. With this in mind,
it was not found reasonable to spend the extensive time to set up and operate the big integrated PIV
system, which is available at DTU. In stead a more primitive system is used, consisting of different
parts modiﬁed and produced in order to create the most versatile PIV system for this project. The
system used for this project consist of a laser, a polycarbonate cylinder, a mirror and a digital camera.
The laser shoot out a continuously beam of light, which is spread out to a laser sheet by the cylinder
as seen in Figure 3.7,
Figure 3.7: A relative primitive PIV set up is used for the purpose of ﬂow visualisation. The laser shoots out
a continuous beam of light which spreads out at the polycarbonate cylinder. A mirror reﬂects the laser sheet
back to the camera.
3.3.1
The Laser sheet
The laser used is a Dantec laser, which used to be part of a Laser Doubler Anemometry (LDA) set-up
but the laser have lost some of its output effect, making it insufﬁcient for LDA measurements. The
output proves to be sufﬁcient for PIV recordings of the type needed for this project. It should be
mentioned that the laser intensity is on the limit of what is possible for the camera to record with
convincing quality. If the light intensity of the reﬂected light had been just a bit less, the camera
would not have been able to record with sufﬁcient shutter speed for the particles to appear as points
rather than traces.
The laser produces a beam of light which have to be spread out to form a sheet. This is done by
shooting through a 6mm clear polished polycarbonate cylinder. To obtain enough width for the sheet
to illuminate the area of interest, the curvature of the cylinder has to be relative big compared to
the diameter of the laser beam. Also by increase the distance from the cylinder to the area to be
illuminated, the laser sheet can be widen.
==PAGE:26==
17
CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL SET-UP
3.3.2
The Camera
The best camera available at the time of the project, is a Nikon D7100 DSLR, which have capability
to shoot video at 60 fps (frames per second) at 720p resolution. When choosing this camera, the
ISO capabilities played a central role. The ISO-number is a measure of the amount of light a digital
camera censor is capable to record and produce an image, i.e the light sensitivity of the censor. It
was found, when doing the preliminary tests of the equipment, that a ISO capability of 4000 to 5000
ISO was needed to capture enough of the reﬂected light from the particles. But high ISO-footage
comes at a cost. The higher the ISO-number, the more noise will be introduced to the image. This
noise can interfere with the recorded particles and result in pour foundation for the PIV analysis. It is
there for of interest to ﬁnd a camera that handles high ISO-values well. The Nikon D7100 does this to
sufﬁciently well but as mentioned, the light reﬂected by the particles could not have been much less
for the camera to produce too mush noise in the images.
The optic used for the recordings is the Zeiss 50mm f/2.0 Macro-Planar ZE. The optical quality of the
optic is very high and it has very low distortion of 0.1% which is a measure that the change of mag-
niﬁcation through out the lens. ( see DxO (2015)). This, together with the possibility of big aperture,
makes it a versatile optic for PIV recordings.
3.3.3
The Particles
The particles chosen for the purpose of studying the ﬂow around the cylinders, is Dantec polyamid
seeding particles with a mean particle diameter of 5 µm. The choice of this particle size is based upon
experience from the preliminary tests of the equipment. Equation (2.21) deﬁnes the needed exposure
time for a particle of this size to be captured as a point rather than a trace. However troubles have
been encountered by getting the particles to spread out in single particles as the tendency is that the
particles lump together in bigger particles. Also the fact that much of the movement is created by the
cylinder moving means that most of the water is moving slowly. From the visual inceptions of the
images it is found that the number of particles appearing as traces are small. As seen in Section 4.2.5
the PIV shows to produce fairly good predictions of the velocities.
3.4
Data processing in MATLAB
All the data processing for this project is done in MATLAB. This Section will go through the data
treatment and features which have been produced to treat the data for both the determination of the
drag- and inertia coefﬁcients as well as the PIV.
3.4.1
Data processing for CD and CM
The output from the load cells and the displacement measurements is in form of volt values. From
calibration of the equipment (See Section 4) the volt signals is converted to loads and displacements
from which the velocity and acceleration also can be found. The raw signal does have some noise
which have to be ﬁltered away in order to get the actual load signal. From the ﬁltered time series the
method described in Section 2.1.5 is applied to determine the force coefﬁcients. MATLAB has a build
in iteration function called fsolve which can be set up to iterate for solutions for multiple equations
where the solution is equal to zero. This have been used for this.
==PAGE:27==
3.4. DATA PROCESSING IN MATLAB
18
Signal ﬁltering
The recorded signals from both force measurements and displacement measurements have all got
noise to a certain degree. Especially the force signal proves to have a great deal of noise. To ﬁlter this
noise away a Hamming ﬁlter is applied. This works as a window function which outside a given user
chosen interval returns zero-values when applied to a signal. Since the Hamming window function is
a bell shaped function, it does have some small transition area where the ﬁltering gradually increase
from zero. The special about the hamming window it that it is designed to make nearest so called side
lobes, the smallest. In practice this means that the frequency band just next to the one of interest is
minimised as much as possible. Section 4.2 veriﬁes the effect of applying the hamming window ﬁlter
by doing a spectral analysis to see how the frequency response for the ﬁltered and unﬁltered signals
compare in the area of interest. It is important that the ﬁlter does not remove any of the signal in the
frequency range of interest.
3.4.2
Data processing for PIV
The PIV software used is by Thielicke and Stamhuis (2014a) which is documented in Thielicke and
Stamhuis (2014b). The software is created as an easy to use PIV software in which multiple images
can be processed to create detailed velocity maps. The images have to be produced in a special way
for this project, at the cylinder moves relative to the camera. This implies a crop of the images to
create the visual impression of the water moving rather than the cylinder. The practical aspect of
this and the calibration is described in Section 4.1.3. This section here is to brieﬂy go through the
process of extraction the images from the video ﬁle and the idea behind algorithm which crops the
images.
A video ﬁle is recorded containing the visualisation of the ﬂow. This ﬁle is loaded into MATLAB
where the individual frames is extracted, converted into gray scale and the contrast and clarity is
enhanced. To crop the images it is necessary to know the displacement in pixels, that the cylinder
moves between every frame (see Equation (4.8)). From this the images are cropped by moving a win-
dow as wide as possible from one side to the other while following the cylinder. It is important to get
the cylinder to stay in the same position in each frame as this is an indication of the right speed is
matched. It is also important that the images are cropped so the pixel size is the same at all times.
Otherwise it will produce wrong velocity perditions.
==PAGE:28==
4
Calibration and Validation
Load cells, displacement recordings and PIV needs calibration to be able to extract usable informa-
tion from the data. This is the ﬁrst step toward producing reliable results. To increase the trustwor-
thiness of the results from the experiment, it is common practice to compare results to prior results
in the literature. The number of experimental investigations upon square cylinders in oscillating
ﬂow are low - the case of oscillatory movement of cylinders through still water, close to not existing.
This chapter will ﬁrst cover the calibration of the equipment and later on a validation experiment
will be set up to conﬁrm the measurements from a single cylinder in relation to data found in the
literature.
4.1
Calibration of equipment
4.1.1
Calibration of carriage and displacement measurement
A more detailed calibration of the carriage can be seen in Appendix A, but a quick overview of the
calibration is given here:
The carriage is controlled by a hydraulic piston as described in Section 3.1.2. The piston is given a
volt signal from the computer, which governs the position. A side mounted LVDT allows one to have
a signal output corresponding to the position of the piston. It was discovered that this LVDT has a
phase lag, which causes misleading results (See Appendix I). As a replacement a draw wire encoder
was used to measure the displacement. These in- and output signals have been calibrated to the
displacement of the piston as shown in Figure 4.1.

When placing the cylinders in the set-up, it is important that the load cells are mounted so the force

to prevent from happening as discussed in Section 3.1.2.
4.1.3
Calibration of PIV system
Calibration of the PIV system consist of distance calibration which determine the equivalent length
of one pixel. The software used for the PIV has an build-in function which allows one to take a ref-
erence image, which deﬁnes a known distance. By using a point and draw tool, the software detect
the number of pixels of the given reference length. By knowing the real world size, the program con-
verts the pixel displacements into meters. The time between the frames for the interrogation sets the
reference time.
But it is not only the displacement of particles which have to be calibrated in this speciﬁc project.
As the camera and the cylinders are moving relative to each other, it is necessary to do a crop of the
video frames in order to make the illusion of the water moving instead of the cylinders. By knowledge
of the displacement amplitude a of the cylinder, the period and the number of frames per second,
it is possible to make an algorithm which crop the frames. The ﬁrst step is to determine the optical
magniﬁcation which is governed by the distance from the camera to the object and the optic it self.
For a given optic, the closer the camera gets, the greater the magniﬁcation. The magniﬁcation is
deﬁned as
M0 =
f
f −d0
(4.7)
where f is the focal length of the optic, i.e. the theoretical distance from the front optic element to the
sensor and d0 is the distance from the front optic element to the object of interest. Figure 4.3 deﬁnes
the distances of the setup, which is used to deﬁne the actual magniﬁcation. As the distance from the
optics to the laser sheet is a total of d0 = (996−51.5)+445 = 1389.5mm where the 51.5mm is the length
of the exact length of optics (see DxO (2015)). This gives the magniﬁcation of M0 = −0.0385.
==PAGE:31==
4.1. CALIBRATION OF EQUIPMENT
22
Figure 4.3: Sketch with measures of the PIV setup for determining the relation between actual displacement
of cylinder and the imaged displacement. All measurements are in millimetres.
From the magniﬁcation it is possible to determine the size of an object as it is projected onto the
photo sensor. The movement of the cylinder only takes place in the x-direction, the displacement in
pixels can be determined by Equation (4.8)
xp(t) = xc(t)·|M0|·
npx
bx
(4.8)
where, xp(t) is the displacement in pixel as function of time, xc(t) is the displacement of the cylinder
as function of time, npx is the number of pixels in the x-direction of the sensor and bx is the width of
the sensor in the x-direction. It is now known how much the cylinder moves in the frame in terms of
pixels.
A displacement series with one or more periods is produced with resolution corresponding to the
frames per second of the video recording. Figure 4.4 deﬁnes the position and velocity as function of
ωt. It is not always the case that the cylinder start at positive displacement, but it will always start to
one of the outer positions and move through the cycle from there.

Some perspective distortion is present due to the fact that the cylinder moves. This cannot be correc-
ted as such, but a longer focal length could be used to minimise this. However, this was not possible
as the space needed for the extra distance between camera and setup was not available. This per-
spective distortion makes it look like the cylinder have moved a bit in the two frames shown in Figure
4.6 but at the laser sheet, the position is almost completely stationary. Some small movements of
the cylinder cannot be avoided as some inaccuracy of the distance measurements can be present as
well as movements caused by the un-smooth movement of the piston driving the cylinder. Also the
real focal length of the optic may vary from the one provided by the manufacture. The best way to
compensate for this is by visual inspection of the cropped frames and ensure that the displacement
of the cylinder at the laser sheet is as close to zero as possible.
4.2
Validation experiment
The purpose of a validation experiment is to make sure that the equipment behaves as expected
and can replicate the results from previous studies. The expected behaviour is that the drag and
inertia coefﬁcients are independent of the Reynolds number and converge for a given number of
periods used to determine CD and CM. Bearman et al. (1984) did an investigation of drag- and inertia
coefﬁcients for bluff bodies such as a square cylinder.
The conditions in the study of Bearman et al. (1984) is a bit different than for the set-up used in this
experiment as a U-tube facility is used to produce oscillating water. A number of square cylinders
==PAGE:33==
4.2. VALIDATION EXPERIMENT
24
with different corner radius is placed one at a time in the center of the U-tube in horizontal position.
The diameter of the cylinders is 2.4 cm, around 60% of the cylinders used in this experiment. The
length is 60.5cm. The natural frequency of the U-tube is T = 3.34s and used for producing oscillatory
ﬂow with KC-number ranging from KC = 1 to KC = 100. The study involved both sharp edged square
cylinders and cylinders with rounded corners. Only the sharp edged cylinders is of interest in this
context. The β-value for the sharp edged cylinders is β = 213 and is held constant due to constant
period, making the Reynold number to vary between about 200 to 2×104 under the assumption that
the drag coefﬁcient is uninﬂuenced by the Reynold number.
This will be used as a reference case in order to validate the experiment set-up .
The validation experiment set-up is as follows: A single cylinder with possibility of measuring in-line
and lift forces. The cylinder is placed far away from the sides of the ﬂume to avoid effects of wall
proximity. The results should not be Re dependent as the cylinders is a square with sharp edges, but
to verify this fully, periods of T = 1s, T = 1.5s and T = 2s is tested. The KC-numbers used are close to
KC = 1,2,4,6,8 and 10. The speciﬁc KC-number will vary a bit. The run time is 5 minutes in order
to produce between at least 150 periods depending on the period. As the ﬁrst thing, a convergence
analysis will show is the number of periods is enough to make the results steady. The water level in
the ﬂume is H = 41.5 cm as standard maximum water level. A lower water level is used but it will
be stated at that point. The distance from the bottom of the ﬂume to the bottom of the cylinder is
h = 0.8 cm.
4.2.1
Time series for validation experiment
As the ﬁrst thing, the time series for the force have been plotted for both the unﬁltered signal and the
ﬁltered signal in order to see the effect of the applied ﬁlter. As an example, the run with KC = 8 for
T = 1 s is used. Figure 4.7 shows four periods from the total time series.

appendix does also show that the eigenfrequency of the cylinder set up is 18.1 Hz, which is far from
the frequencies used in the experiments.

analysis as well as all the KC-values. The full overview can be seen in Table 4.1. Note that the values
listed in Table 4.1 is the expected values based on the desired KC value. As the mass of the carriage is
not that small, it causes the displacements to be larger. Especially for the lowest period of T = 1s it is
noticeable for the highest KC values. In general the KC-values will be refereed to as KC = 1, 2, 4, 6, 8
and 10 but the actual value may be different. The correct values will be used for plots and Table 4.2,
4.3 and 4.4 for the actual values for each period.

It is clear that the drag coefﬁcients for KC = 1 and KC = 2 is having a hard time to converge, but
the rest is converged after the ﬁrst 100 periods. The smallest KC numbers are produced with very
small velocities which is used in the Morrison prediction to produce the CD coefﬁcients. The force
==PAGE:36==
27
CHAPTER 4. CALIBRATION AND VALIDATION
signal is simply not strong enough to give a consistent estimate of the drag force. It is concluded
that if convergence have not occurred after 300 periods, it is unlikely that it will happen with in a
reasonable amount of periods.
The inertia coefﬁcients all converge to a relative steady level after around 100 periods. Again the two
lowest KC-numbers tend to be a bit unsteady. The reason to this is due to the same as for the drag
coefﬁcients, the force signal is simply not strong enough to produce fully consistent results, but as
seen for the higher KC-numbers a general tendency is that both CD and Cm is a bit unsteady even
for high number of evaluated periods. However, it should be noted that the ﬂuctuations in both CD
and Cm is relative small after the ﬁrst 100 periods (except for the two ﬁrst KC-numbers).

Again, staring with the convergence of CD, it seen that the lowest KC-values is a bit unsteady, but not
worse than the previous ones. As a matter of fact, the convergence of KC = 2 is the best yet, but then
KC = 3 is a bit more unsteady. The same goes for the higher KC-values which again seems to have
converged fairly at around period number 100.
The inertia coefﬁcients follow the same pattern as the previous two experiments with fair conver-
gence after 100 periods.
The convergence analysis shows that when comparing the ﬁrst 100 periods all three experiments
seems to converge fairly. It is seen that for the lowest KC values it can be difﬁcult to get convergence,
especially for the two longest periods, which also is the ones which produces the smallest load signals
as the velocities are smaller than for the one second period experiment. There for it may be period of
T = 1 second, which yields the most robust results.
Small ﬂuctuations in the results, can introduce a bit of randomness in predicting the force coefﬁ-
cients. To even this uncertainty out, a mean of the results for a given range of period number can be
used as the concluding value to the coefﬁcient. This is done in Section 4.2.3.
As the sampling time is the same ﬁve minutes for all three experiments, they have different maximum
number of periods, but all have reached approximately same level of convergence after around 100
periods. Non the less, a longer time series will produce more reliable results after all, which again
==PAGE:38==
29
CHAPTER 4. CALIBRATION AND VALIDATION
makes the period of T = 1 second the favourite to use in the further investigation. However, this is
only true if it can be proven that the results are independent of changes in Reynolds number. It will
be showed, that this is the case, in the next section.
4.2.3
CD and CM for validation experiment
To conﬁrm the Reynold number independence and to relate the found drag- and inertia coefﬁcients
to the literature, all the coefﬁcients from the three experiments have been plotted in Figure 4.12. The
inertia coefﬁcients are including the Froude-Krylov force which is done by adding one to Cm.
A correction is made to the values from Bearman et al. (1984) because they use the area of the circle
within the square as the area so Ac = π
4 D2. For the results presented in the report, the actual area
of the cylinders are used as it makes more sense to talk about the actual area of the cylinders. The
correction needed to be applied for the inertia coefﬁcients are as follows:
The inertia force is determined as
Fi = ρCM A
(4.9)
As the actual ﬂow force is the same, CM will with change according to the choice of area. Thus the
following equation can be written
ρCM,c Ac = ρCM,s As
(4.10)
where subscript c is for the circle area and s is for the square area. The relation between the two area
are
Ac
As
= π/4D2
D2
= π
4
(4.11)
which also is the relation between CM,c and CM,s as
CM,s = CM,c · π
4
(4.12)
This correction have been done for all CM values from Bearman et al. (1984).
In order to eliminate the uncertainty, which are introduced by the ﬂuctuations described in Section
4.2.2, the mean values of the results have been taken for evaluations between period number 120
and 140. As it was observed that a more or less consistent drag- and inertia coefﬁcient was obtained
after the 100th period, it seems fair to use from period 120 to determine the coefﬁcients. By this the
concluding values of the drag- and inertia coefﬁcient will be based upon 20 evaluations with 120 to
140 included periods. The reason why only 140 periods are used at the maximum number of periods,
at this is as many the experiment with T = 2 contains.
Figure 4.12 shows the obtained force coefﬁcients for the three validation experiments.

Figure 4.12: CD and CM for a single square cylinder at periods of 1, 1.5 and 2 seconds. The KC-vaules in the

First thing to notice is that the KC-values have not been completely matched by the machinery. This
is difﬁcult to predict precisely how mush the carriage is going to move, especially for the low periods,
as the mass of the carriage force the piston to displace more than predicted.
When looking at the drag coefﬁcients, the tendency is that from KC-values from four and above,
the results produced in this validation experiment coincide with the ones obtained by Bearman et al.
(1984), except for KC = 4 which for T = 2 s is a bit below the others. For the lower KC-values it is more
scattered. This complies with the tendency observed in the convergence analysis in Section 4.2.2
which found that convergence could not be obtained even after 300 periods. If one took the values
for the two last KC-values and took the mean, it would approach the line from Bearman et al. (1984)
which, if the original article is consulted, also is based on rather scattered data points for KC = O(1).
Overall, the drag set-up is capable of determine valid drag coefﬁcients, at least for KC-numbers from
four and above.
The tendency for the inertia coefﬁcients is, in terms of scatter, the same as for the drag coefﬁcients.
Only the two lowest KC-numbers are a bit scattered, but the inertia coefﬁcients for KC = 4 and KC =
6 is very close to the ones from Bearman et al. (1984). For the highest two KC-numbers i.e KC = 8
and KC = 10, it seems that the values obtained from this experiment is a bit below what the reference
study obtain. This may be the effect of the cylinders not being fully submerged into the water, which
can cause disturbances at the bottom and at the top. At the top of the cylinder a surface wave is
generated as a result of the cylinder moving. This is not a big problem in open water, but when the
channel is narrowed down to the 28 cm, the cylinder suddenly takes up a relative large space of the
full channel width. This is an effective wave generator, especially for the cases of several cylinders.
This will be further elaborated upon in Section 4.2.4.
Over all, the validation experiment yields a trustworthy picture of the force coefﬁcients at KC ran-
ging from 1 to 10 for the single cylinder in open water. When the cylinder is placed in the channel,
the surface wave becomes signiﬁcant and has to be taken care of, which Section 4.2.4 deals with.
The Reynolds number independence gives the opportunity to chose the period T = 1s for faster ob-
taining large data series in order to make the results as robust as possible.

The tendency is the same as for the test with maximum water depth, presented in Figure 4.12. The

plained by the a lower signal due to smaller forces on the cylinder. Other wise it does not show any
clear tendency. The only thing, which vaguely can be observed, is that the results could be a bit de-
pendent on the period, i.e. Reynolds dependency. The boundary effects at the top and bottom would
natural be more pronounced as the relationship between the water depth and the distance of which
the boundary effects are affecting the cylinder, is smaller. It is for the remainder of the project chosen
to use H = 41.5 cm as it seams to produce the most consistent results, regardless of the Reynolds
number.
4.2.4
Suppression of surface wave
The validation experiment is carried out for a single cylinder with over half a meter to the sides of the
ﬂume. The impact of the generated wave at the top of the cylinder is relative low, but still present.
Especially on CM it becomes viable as the values are a bit lower than for the Bearman et al. (1984)
results. From the experiments in the conﬁned channel and with more cylinders, this wave becomes
quite signiﬁcant. Again the CM is inﬂuenced but in a much more signiﬁcant way. The wave builds up
in front of the cylinders due to the pressure the cylinder acts upon the water. As the cylinder stops to
go the other way the hydrostatic pressure difference, caused by the wave, acts on the cylinders in the
same direction as the acceleration. This counteracts the force from the acceleration, thus decrease
CM, as the acceleration is at its maximum. For the case where the cylinder is moving at maximum
velocity and the acceleration is zero, the same phenomenon is present, but the effect is the opposite
- the drag coefﬁcient is being increased by the presents of the wave. It should be noted that the wave
is generated by the pressure on the face of the cylinder, and it is the hydrostatic pressure difference
which will create a pressure contribution. The effect on CM is expected to be bigger than for CD,
since the drag force is relative big at maximum velocity - especially at high KC-values the effect of CD
is the smaller. See Figure 4.14 for visual explanation.
==PAGE:41==
4.2. VALIDATION EXPERIMENT
32
Figure 4.14: To the right: The cylinder is at its peak velocity and the wave is being generated as a result of the
pressure. To the left: The cylinder have just stopped and accelerates to the right. The wave created from the
cylinder moving to the left, will act instantaneous on the cylinder as seen in the ﬁgure
A solution to this problem can be putting a lid on the surface of the water, which will suppress the
generated surface wave around the cylinder. The plate will be mounted on the carriage so it will
follow the movement of the cylinder. A hole in the middle with just enough space around the cylinder
will ensure that no interaction between the lid and the cylinder will take place. The principal can be
seen in Figure 4.15
Figure 4.15: The cylinder have just stopped and accelerates to the right. The lid is mounted so it follows the
movement of the cylinder, thus creating the same suppression of the wave at all places relative to the cylinder.
The effect of the lid is that the surface wave is minimised. The lid have only been installed in the
narrowed channel with a blocking ratio of γ = 0.143. The effect of the lid is presented and discussed
in Section 5.1.1. A short investigation of the period independence of the lid was made to see if the
period did change the effect of the suppressed wave. This sees not to be the case as seen in Appendix
G.
==PAGE:42==
33
CHAPTER 4. CALIBRATION AND VALIDATION
4.2.5
Validation of PIV
As well as for the force coefﬁcients, it is necessary to investigate whether the PIV setup is capable
of producing reliable results. For this case, a single cylinder is placed in the narrowed channel and
oscillated back and forth at KC = 10.05 with a peak velocity of Um = 0.134 m/s. The camera and
light intensity of the laser requires relative slow velocities, why a period of T = 3s is chosen. A way of
determining whether PIV is able to predict the velocities a time series of the the mean ﬂow velocity
over several periods. As the frames are cropped to compensate for the camera not moving along with
the set up govern the velocity which is used as theoretical velocity of the oscillating movement. The
camera is set to record in 60 frames per second.
An test recording with a single cylinder is performed where eight periods of motion is recorded and
analysed in PIVLab with two different frame rates to see the effect.
For a 30 fps analysis the interrogation area chosen is a 64×64 pixel area which is moved in steps of
16 pixels. The choice of interrogation area size is based upon the remedy that the displacement of
the particles should be in the order of 1/4 of the size of the area in combination of the fact that a
bigger area yields a more robust interrogation. It is found that the 64×64 is a good balance of these
two when using 30 fps. If a smaller window is chosen, the scatter of the predicted velocities tend to
become very large. A bigger window will reduce scatter, but the resolution of the ﬂow structures will
suffer.
If a smaller window is needed the frame rate need to be 60 fps. in order to decrease the particle
displacement. A too large displacement will result in the particle moving out of the interrogation
area and correlation is lost. A 40 × 40 interrogation areal seems appropriate as the highest particle
displacements can be up to 10 pixels per frame in the highest gap velocities for the 1x2 constellations
later on.
No image corrections have been applied in the software, but a conversion to black and white and an
increase of the contrast have been applied to the images before loaded into PIVLab.
Figure 4.16 shows the velocity map of the ﬂow at time ωt = 90 for a 30 fps. analysis. Note that the
cylinder is not placed in the middle of the channel. This is not the case in reality, but the structure of
the ﬂume blocks about 3 cm of the total width at the bottom of the image.

34
Mean velocity validation
The ﬁrst step to verify the PIV setup is to have a look on the mean velocity in a cross section away
from the cylinder. This section is marked to the left in Figure 4.16. The mean velocity should be close
or equal to the velocity determined by the crop applied to the frames, as the water is stationary. Small
velocities from the vortices and disturbances from the cylinder will affect the water, thus the mean is
chosen as the most robust measure. Figure 4.17 shows the result
In general it seems to be a god ﬁt between the PIV predicted velocity and the actual theoretical velo-
city determined by the cropping. This indicates that the calibration of the PIV it self is good, as well
as the cropping of the frames. Where the velocity gradient is small i.e. at the maximum velocities,
the PIV is a bit ambiguous about the velocity. This is because the change in velocity from frame to
frame is not always big enough to cause a full pixel displacement each time. This makes the predicted
PIV velocity to jump in-between two to three velocities. Not much can be done about this with the
resolution available. One could move the camera closer, but this would comprise the ability to see
the ﬂow structures around the cylinder. Also for the case of multiple cylinders, the distance of the
cylinders requires the camera to be place where it is, in order to capture the whole scene.

Vector validation and rejection of outliers
As the PIV analysis is based upon a statistical correlation analysis it can produce false vectors or out-
liers which are not representative of the actual ﬂow. A visual inspection of the velocities in a scatter
plot is provided by the PIV software from which the user can mark which values it to be rejected. The
general scatter of from the applied PIV analysis is fairly small. A typical vector scatter plot is shown
in Figure 4.22. The red square shows the vectors which are chosen to be valid. The vectors outside
this selection will be rejected and new values will be interpolated from the nearby vectors.
Figure 4.22: Screen shot from PIVlab, the software used for the PIV analysis. The red marking is the vectors
chosen to be valid. The vectors outside this area will be rejected.
==PAGE:47==
4.3. CONCLUDING THE CALIBRATION AND VALIDATION EXPERIMENT
38
4.3
Concluding the calibration and validation experiment
This chapter shows the methods of calibrations for load cells and for displacement measures. These
calibrations have been used to collect reliable data from the validation experiment, where a single
square cylinder have been placed in free water and oscillated back and forth for KC-values at around
1,2,4,6,8 and 10. Convergence in the force coefﬁcients is found above 100 periods in general. The
results for the force coefﬁcients CD and CM is compared to results from Bearman et al. (1984) and
shows general good coincidence. For the lowest KC values a bit of scatter is present due to small
force signal. The conclusion of the convergence analysis and the validation experiment leads to the
choice of using the T = 1 second period. 200 periods will seems appropriate to produce reliable force
measurements at this period.
A surface wave have been observed and a method to suppress is by putting a lid on the water surface.
The effect of this is seen in Section 5.1 and in Section 5 in general.
For the PIV setup, a velocity prediction over several periods shows good behaviour if the 30 fps ana-
lysis is used. It may have trouble record velocity measures close to the cylinders, why a 60 analysis
have been tried out as well. The 60 frames analysis does not perform as well as the 30 fps analysis
when it comes to determining the velocity, but it is better to detect velocities close to the cylinder.
This shows a general issue about PIV where velocity gradients are present. The PIV is best for rel-
ative steady ﬂows where the settings can be ﬁne tuned to a smaller span of velocities than what is
the case of an oscillatory ﬂow. Some velocity proﬁles have also been extracted and showing reas-
onable velocity predictions across the ﬂow width both for cross section through and away from the
cylinder.
==PAGE:48==
5
Results - force measurements
The results for the force coefﬁcients obtained from the constellations 1x1, 1x2, 2x1 and 2x2 are presen-
ted in Section 5.1, 5.2, 5.4 and 5.6 respectively. All drag- and inertia coefﬁcients can also be seen in
Appendix D in Table form. Also the mean forces and time series will be used to describe the force
development for the 1x2, 2x1 and 2x2 constellations in Section 5.3, 5.5 and 5.7 respectively. Appendix
H sums up the forces experienced by the waves generated and reﬂected by the ﬂume. All the force
measurements consists of data from 200 periods, with the period T = 1s.
The results will be compared to the numerical model from Bjø rke (2015) which have all the set
ups modelled in an OpenFOAM model. The results from Bjø rke (2015) have all been corrected by
multiplying CM with π/4 in order to make the area correction, as the area used by Bjø rke (2015) is
A = D2(π/4). This was the same as for the validation experiment in Section 4.2.3. To clarify the dir-
ection of forces relative to their signs are deﬁned in Figure 5.1 below. This is mostly for the sections
regarding the mean forces.
Figure 5.1: Deﬁnition of positive in-line and lift forces and velocity as well
Lastly in Section 5.9 the total forces on the simple porous structure of 2x2 cylinder is presented and
compared to the force predictions by the Morrison approach with free stream values and values cor-
rected according to Det Norske Veritas guidelines, see Dnv (2011).
The results for the ﬂow visualisation is presented in Section 6. The connection between the force
measurements and the ﬂow visualisation is made in Section 7.
5.1
1x1 - single cylinder
The set-up of 1x1 cylinder in conﬁned channel serves mostly as a reference experiment. Both to see
the effect of the conﬁnement relative to the free cylinder and in relation to the more complex set-ups.
Figure 5.2 shows the set-up. Both the situation with lid on top of the water and without are presented
and discussed in this section.

The force coefﬁcients with the lid in Figure 5.3 is almost fully in agreement with the results obtained
from the validation experiment which is the same case but unconﬁned. The blockage effect does
not seem to create any change to the force coefﬁcients for blockage ratio at γ = 0.143. If the article
from Okajima et al. (1997) is studied, the ﬁgure describing the change of CD is in agreement with
this result. Figure 5.4 show the ﬁgure from the above mentioned article. The (b) part of the ﬁgure is
concerning the oscillating cylinder.
==PAGE:50==
