CHAPTER 5. RESULTS - FORCE MEASUREMENTS
Figure 5.4: Figure from Okajima et al. (1997) (modiﬁed) showing the change in mean drag coefﬁcient for a
quadratic square cylinder. The bottom (b) ﬁgure presents variation in the mean drag coefﬁcient with changing
frequencies. The study have been conducted under constant Reynolds number, but with varying amplitudes,
thus varying KC-numbers. Marked with red is the blockage ratio of interest where it is seen that the drag
coefﬁcients have not changed signiﬁcantly
From Figure 5.4 it can be seen that the blockage effect for approx γ = 0.15 is very close the give the
same drag coefﬁcients as for an unconﬁned ﬂow environment. This supports that force measure-
ments from the singe cylinder with the lid, is under no inﬂuence of blockage. However, Okajima et al.
(1997) does not mention anything about the inertia coefﬁcient and how this develops as result to
blockage.
If looking at Figure 5.3 the results from Bjø rke (2015) is presented as well but all numerical results
are above the measured values.
The drag coefﬁcients are, for the highest KC-values, in relative good agreement but for the lowest
KC-values the deviation is much higher. The lowest KC-values are inertia dominated and the drag
coefﬁcients are hard to predict with high accuracy.
The numerical results from the inertia coefﬁcients are placed above the ones found from the present
experiment but follows the same development as the experimental results. The reason for this is not
clear but for the following 1x2, 2x1 and 2x2 set ups the tendency is the same. Some of the deviation
may be attributed to the fact that the surface wave can not been suppressed totally, which cause the
inertia coefﬁcients to drop.

For CD is the values for the conﬁned cylinder is higher than for the unconﬁned cylinder. This is not
seen for the case with the lid on, which was presented in Figure 5.3b. The reason for this can be found
in the the generated wave as described in Section 4.2.4 which increase the drag coefﬁcients. This
wave is naturally also present for the unconﬁned case, but the wave is not as big as for the conﬁned
channel. When the cylinder is placed in the channel the cylinder starts to work as an effective wave
generator as the volume of the cylinder have increased relative to the channel width.
When looking at the inertia coefﬁcients the measured values without the lid shows a tendency which
is close to the unconﬁned measurement. A slight deviation is found which is expected to be the
surface wave which is bigger due to the conﬁnement.

the results from Bjø rke (2015) is higher for the lowest KC-values and a bit smaller for the highest KC-
values. The surface wave does increase the drag coefﬁcient so with that in mind the results makes
sense, as the drag coefﬁcients have increased relative to the case with out the lid. The inertia coef-
ﬁcients from the experiment is in general below the values from Bjø rke (2015) and with a higher
deviation than for the case with lid on. The surface wave does have an impact as the inertia coefﬁ-
cients have dropped a bit relative to the lid-case.

5.2
1x2 - Two side by side cylinders
The ﬁrst multi cylinder case treated is the 1x2 set up which is sketched in Figure 5.6 where also num-

Figure 5.6: 1x2 constellation in 0.28m channel. The top cylinder is cylinder one and the bottom one is cylinder
2. both the cylinders have the diameter D = 40mm.
The 1x2 set up is having the characteristics presented in Table 5.1. The expected behaviour of this
set up, is that the gap between the two cylinders create a jet-like ﬂow, especially for the lowest gap
ratios. This will enhance the forces acting on the cylinders. As the set up is symmetric it will also be
expected that the forces are roughly equal for both cylinders.

Figure 5.7: CD and CM for 1x2 set up with lid. The not ﬁlled markers are cylinder 1 and the ﬁlled markers are
cylinder 2 cf. Figure 5.6
If looking at the drag coefﬁcients the over all tendency is, that they follow same pattern as the single
cylinder but with increased drag force. The gap-ratio with the highest drag force is for d/D = 0.5
which is followed by the gap-ratio of d/D = 1 and the lowest drag forces are for the d/D = 2 gap ratio.
Only one point is acting against the general tendency, namely the CD value for KC = .22 at d/D = 1,
which is placed above the drag coefﬁcient for d/D = 0.5. Why this is the case, is not supported by
any other tendency in the data but, but the same is found in the numerical model by Bjø rke (2015),
see Section 5.2.4). The fact that the drag coefﬁcients increase with decreasing gap-ratios are linked
to the ﬂow speed up in between the cylinders. A smaller gap will generate a bigger velocity through
the space between the cylinders. The ﬂow visualisation in Section 6.1 will discus this. However, an
observation is made for the smallest gap-ratio as will be explained in a moment
Regarding the inertia coefﬁcient, the tendency is the same as for the drag coefﬁcients, but with much
smaller effect for increasing gap-ratio. The two biggest gap-ratios yields almost the same magnitude
for CM, where only the d/D = 0.5 is increased signiﬁcantly. Comparing to the single cylinder, CM for
the ﬁrst two gap-ratios do not show signiﬁcantly effect of the increased velocity. If one think of where
the biggest contribution to CM is taking place, it is where the velocity is zero, or close to zero. For
the two widest gaps, the effect of the gap is not that big. For the smallest gap-ratio, the gap may be
so small that before the ﬂow develops the cylinders may to work more as a singe wide block, before
the water is pressed through the gap. This is also indicated by the PIV analysis which shows that the
water has a tendency of being pushed around the two cylinders as discussed in Section 7.1.1.

the lid, is plotted for reference.

The drag coefﬁcients do follow the same trend as for the case with the lid, when looking at the val-
ues for the KC > 2.2 where the drag coefﬁcients increase with decreasing gap-ratio.. The biggest
difference is that the values are slightly higher. The reason for this is the wave acting as a positive
contribution to the drag force. For the lowest KC-values the drag coefﬁcients deviate quite allot from
the results found with the lid (see Figure 5.7). Since the lowest KC-numbers are inertia dominated,
these drag coefﬁcients are associated with some uncertainty. Once again it is observed that the gap-
ratio of d/D = 1 produces the highest drag coefﬁcient for KC = 2.2. This was also found for the case
with the lid.
For the inertia coefﬁcients the decreasing gap-ratio increase the magnitude of CM but the presents
of the surface wave is clear: The gap-ratios of d/D = 2 and dy/D = 1 yields inertia coefﬁcients which
are below the ones for a single cylinder. This is against the intuitive assumption that the two cylinder
generate higher blockage, thus higher velocities, which will increase the force coefﬁcients. Also the
jet-like ﬂow between the cylinder should further increase the coefﬁcients compared to a single cyl-
inder. This seems true for the drag coefﬁcients, but apparently not for the inertia coefﬁcients, when
looking at the case with out the lid. The explanation for this counter intuitive behaviour is described
in Section 4.2.4 but to recap the effect is that CM is decreased due to the wave. This can cause the
inertia coefﬁcients to decrease a great amount relative to the case with the lid.
5.2.3
The effect of gap-ratio and blockage
As the cylinder have their distance in between them changed, so does the blockage when looking
at the full structure. The bigger the gap ratio, the bigger the full structure blockage. The effect of
higher of blockage will result in higher force coefﬁcients, but as the gap increase with the blockage,
the real blockage effect may be closer to that two single cylinder produce, rather than what the hole
structure inclusive the gap suggests. This can be seen in both Figure 5.7 and 5.8 where clearly the
force coefﬁcients increase when the gap-ratio and full structure blockage decrease. To get a good
overview of how the gap-ratio inﬂuence the force coefﬁcients, Figure 5.9 have been produced for
the case with the 1x2 set up with the lid on and Figure 5.10 for the case without the lid. The force
coefﬁcients used in the plots are the mean value for the two cylinders.

for the case with and without the lid exists, as Figure 5.10b and 5.12b shows. A bit more scatter for the
case without the lid is observed, but not much. For both cases the change from d/D = 2 to d/D = 1
gives a nearly no change in magnitude of CM. Only when the gap-ratio is decreased to 0.5 the effect
becomes clear. As mentioned before, this can be attributed to the fact that the amount of water
which is moved at around U = 0 is not signiﬁcant changing before the spacing between the cylinders
becomes so close that they start to act like a single coherent structure.

5.3
Forces for 1x2 set up
The forces acting on the 1x2 set up is more than just the force coefﬁcients which describes the rela-
tions between force magnitude, velocity and acceleration. This section is to give an overview of the
mean forces acting on the cylinders. All the time series across the gap-ratios and KC-numbers have
similar time series. There for only a few time series will be presented as examples.
==PAGE:59==

where the inertia force from the mass of the cylinder have been subtracted.

Figure 5.15: In-line and lift force for the 1x2 set up at gap-ratio 0.5 for the case with the lid on at KC = 11.3.
The inertia of the cylinder have been subtracted from the in-line forces.
The forces are as expected very close to be the same for both the cylinders. In the time series at peak
force some slight variation is present between the two cylinders. In theory for a perfect undisturbed
oscillating ﬂow the in-line force for the 1x2 constellation should be symmetric. This seems not to be
the case as seen in the section about the mean forces in Section 5.3.1.
The lift force have also been recorded for the two cylinders and can be seen in Figure 5.15b for
KC = 11.3 at d/D = 0.5. The lift force indicates that a symmetric force action is going on, but it
seems like the actual vortex shedding is not what the ﬁgure show as all the peaks are pointing the
same way indicating vortex shedding to the same side of the two cylinders. This is not observed in
the ﬂow visualisation. In stead the cylinders may be under inﬂuence of the fact that it is not sup-
ported in both ends which makes it a bit unstable. This will enhance any disturbances from miss
alignments or bad carriage movement. The lift forces from the vortex shedding are very small so any
disturbance can result in false lift forces. There for the lift forces may not be representative of the
actual physics of an oscillating ﬂow as too many unknowns can have affected the results. The results
may be presented anyway in Appendix E as it may reveal some lift force tendency but it should be
used with caution.
The effect of the waves generated in general have been measured and presented in Appendix H. The
maximum waves have been recorded after a KC = 11.3 run.
5.3.1
Mean forces for 1x2 set up
As mentioned a perfect oscillating ﬂow would be expected to yield symmetric in-line forces for the
1x2 set up. This is not entirely the case as some asymmetry shows up for all three cases of gap-ratio.
Figure 5.16 shows the ensemble average for the force time series for 1x2 set up d/D = 0.5 for KC = 11.3
over 180 periods

It is observed that the two force measurements follow each other very well where the velocity increase
e.g. for positive acceleration. When the acceleration becomes negative the two force measurements
starts to deviate from each other with cylinder two having the largest force. The reason for this is
unclear but due to the issues with alignment it could cause a skew in the measurements. If it is
caused by the interaction between the cylinders is there for not evident.
This can very well be the case for d/D = 1 as well which is seen in Figure 5.17 where the same
is observed. The difference is smaller now and especially for the ﬁrst half period the difference is
small.

5.4
2x1 - Two cylinders in tandem arrangement
The 2x1 set up is sketched in Figure 5.19 where the cylinders are numbered to keep track of the
forces.
Figure 5.19: 2x1 constellation in 0.28m channel. The left cylinder is cylinder one and the right one is cylinder
2
The expected behaviour of this set up is, that the cylinders will shield for each other in turn so the
overall force coefﬁcients will be smaller than for a single cylinder. The characteristics of the set up
can be seen in Table 5.2.

5.4. 2X1 - TWO CYLINDERS IN TANDEM ARRANGEMENT
54
The shielding effect is quite obvious for the drag coefﬁcient where is it seen that the closer the cylin-
ders are spaced, the more shielding is going on and the lower the drag coefﬁcient. For the gap-ratio
of d/D = 2 the drag coefﬁcients are almost the same as for a single cylinder. The tendency for a
single cylinder is is repeated for all gap-ratios giving the impression that the shielding effect is close
to linear. This will be commented further upon in Section 5.4.3.
For the inertia coefﬁcient the effect is the same, where a decrease in gap-ratio yields a decrease in
force coefﬁcient. For the gap-ratio of d/D = 0.5 the development follows almost the same curve as
the singe cylinder, which also is the case for the d/D = 1 gap-ratio. For the biggest gap-ratio the
development starts in the same way as the others, but after KC = 4.5 the inertia coefﬁcient drops
a bit from the general path. The last point for this gap-ratio shows a bit deviation between the two
cylinders where cylinder two is lies a bit over cylinder one.
In general the interaction between the cylinders are very small for the widest gap-ratio of d/D = 2.
This supports what Chern et al. (2010) also concluded, that for d/D = 2 no interaction occurs as
such.

Figure 5.21: CD and CM for the 2x1 constellation for the case without the lid. The not ﬁlled markers are cylinder

The general tendency for the drag coefﬁcient is roughly the same for the case without the lid, but
for the biggest gap-ratio for the two smallest KC-numbers, the drag coefﬁcient is bigger than for the
single cylinder case. This is a bit counter intuitive, but then thinking about the effect of the surface
wave described in Section 4.2.4, it may have a natural cause. The surface wave will increase the drag
coefﬁcient compared to a ﬂat water surface. As two cylinder may create a bigger surface wave than
a single cylinder, the effect for the two cylinders will be bigger than for a single cylinder. This makes
the drag coefﬁcients to exceed the ones for the single cylinder.
The inertia coefﬁcients for the 2x1 constellation with out the lid shows that the shielding effect is
increasing with decreasing gap-ratio as expected. The biggest gap-ratio is not as close to the single
cylinder as for the case with the lid, but again the surface wave makes it difﬁcult to compare the two
results directly.

The drag coefﬁcients for the case with the lid increase very close to linear as the gap-ratio is increased.

wave, which affect the force coefﬁcients.
The inertia coefﬁcients for both the case with the lid and without the lid show very little change as
the gap-ratio is changed. It shows that the shielding effect for the inertia coefﬁcient does not depend
as much on the gap-with as the drag coefﬁcient does.

with the gap-ratio.

looking at Figure 5.24b which again indicates force development close to the one found for a single
cylinder. Only KC = 1.1 is a bit off relative to the other KC-numbers in this investigation. The normal-
ised inertia coefﬁcients are also showing signs of the shielding effect being accelerated for increasing
gap-ratio just as the drag coefﬁcient did but with much less effect.

For the 2x1 case with out the lid, the scatter for both the drag coefﬁcient and the inertia coefﬁcient

with the gap ratio, showing signs of the two cylinders starts have same force development as a single
cylinder terms of drag coefﬁcient. In contrast to the 1x2 set up in Section 5.2.3 the wave which is
generated by the 2x1 set up is quite like the wave for a single cylinder because the blockage ratio is
the same and the area of the cylinder which generate the wave is close to the area of a single cylinder.
This makes the scatter less signiﬁcant especially for the smallest gap-ratios.
The inertia coefﬁcient show roughly the same tendency as Figure 5.23b does but with a bit more
scatter. The general tendency is still that a decrease in gap-ratio produces smaller inertia coefﬁcients.
No other general tendency can be derived from Figure 5.25b.

the case with the lid.

in general looks very close to the rest of the measured time series for the 2x1 constellation. Since it
is two cylinder moving in same line the force series is not expected symmetric as such as shielding
effect may occur as indicated in the force coefﬁcients.

For the in-line force the time series for the two cylinders are not the same all over as expected. The
shielding effect makes one of the cylinder forces to grow bigger at one half period than the next.
This is a bit hard to see in the ﬁgure above as the ﬂow patter can create random force development.
In stead an ensemble averaged force representation have been produced as seen in the next sec-
tion.
For the lift-forces the force series are showing that the lift peaks happens at the same time for both
the cylinder and that they point in the same direction in general. Not much more can be said with
certainty due to the issues with measuring the lift forces accurate. In principal the lift forces should
be very much alike for the two cylinders as symmetric vortex shedding is expected.
The effect of the waves generated in general have been measured and presented in Appendix H. The
maximum waves have been recorded after a KC = 11.3 run .
5.5.1
Mean forces for 2x1 set up
The lift force will not be touched upon in this section as the quality of the measurement makes it
hard to say anything certain about them. They are simply too small to be valid lift forces. The mean
lift force are presented in Appendix E in Table E.22 and E.24 the lift forces with and without the lid
respectively, for the sake of documentation.
==PAGE:69==
5.5. FORCES FOR 2X1 SET UP
60
The ensemble averaged force time series for the in-line force have have been produced for all gap
ratios a KC = 11.3 for the sake to illustrate the effect of interaction between the two cylinders. Figure
5.29 below show the force development for d/D = 0.5. Cylinder 1 is upstream for positive velocities
for reference.
0

t [s]
Fin [N]
 
 
cyl. 1
cyl. 2
U, not to scale
Figure 5.29: 2x1 set up. Ensemble average over 180 periods for both cylinders at KC = 11.3 for d/D = 0.5
Apparently the force magnitude for both cylinders are bigger when they are placed down stream.
From intuition the expected behaviour would be that the shielding effect would make the force
biggest on the upstream cylinder but this is not the case. The force time series mean values con-
ﬁrm this as seen in Table E.21 but only with values slightly bigger than what just could be insecurity
on the measurements. The reason for this is believed to be the water in the gap, which is stagnated
between the cylinder. As the velocity is low relative to the ﬂow surrounding the cylinders the pressure
may be bigger due to the Bernoulli principal. This lowers the overall force on the cylinder then it is
placed upstream. It does also act upon the down stream cylinder which then experience a bigger net
force than the upstream cylinder as the wake is acting as well, thus the force time series in Figure
5.29. The effect seems to be bigger for the ﬁrst half period but this may be due to the trouble with
alignment.
It is worth mentioning that the same phenomenon was observed by Bjø rke (2015).
Looking at the d/D = 1 case for the 2x1 set up the same phenomena appears to be the case but
with much smaller impact as seen in Figure 5.30. Again the upstream cylinder shows a higher force
magnitude than the down stream but the effect is almost non-existent. The over all magnitude of
the force have increased as well just as the force coefﬁcients in Section 5.4.1 shows. This means the
upstream cylinder also experience a higher over force than for the gap-ratio of d/D = 0.5. The gap-
width have increased making the gap-ﬂow velocities to increase. This may cause a pressure drop
relative to the case of d/D = 0.5 and lowering the counter pressure. At the same time gap is wide
enough for some of the surrounding ﬂow to interact with the down stream cylinder so all in all the
force on both cylinder are close to being equal. Looking at the calculated mean values for the cylinder
they are very small for both cylinders. Any slightest interference in the mean position of the cylinder
will change sign on both of them.

5.6. 2X2 - FOUR CYLINDERS AS A POROUS STRUCTURE
62
5.6
2x2 - Four cylinders as a porous structure
The 2x2 set up is sketched in Figure 5.32. The set up is in principal a simple large scale porous struc-
ture of a symmetric kind. The forces are in theory equally distributed among the cylinders, but this
not necessarily entirely true the case when doing an experiment. To present the force coefﬁcients in
a foreseeable way, the force coefﬁcient are presented as the mean value of the four cylinders along
with the standard deviation.
Figure 5.32: 2x2 constellation in 0.28m channel
The expected behaviour of the development of the force coefﬁcients is a mix between what was the
case for the 1x2 and 2x1 set up in Section 5.2 and 5.4 respectively. To give an overview of the constel-
lation of 2x2 cylinders, Table 5.3 is presenting the characteristics.
Table 5.3: Experiment overview. The constellation is X x Y direction acording to Figure 3.3.
Constellation
Width b [mm]

Blockage Dy/b

5.6.1
CD and CM for 2x2 with lid
In Figure 5.33 the drag- and inertia coefﬁcients are plotted as function of the KC-numbers. The
dashed line is the case of a single cylinder with the lid for comparison. The lines represent mean
values plotted with the standard deviation along with it. For the drag coefﬁcient the development
for all three gap-ratios have the same overall shape, which is very close to the shape of the single
cylinder. The biggest gap-ratio of d/D = 2 yields the biggest drag coefﬁcients like the case of the 2x1
constellation. The standard deviation is very little for all KC-numbers except the case of KC = 1.1
where a bit scatter between the four cylinders creates a bigger standard deviation. The gap-ratio of
d/D = 1 almost collapse with the line for a single cylinder. Both shielding and blockage are taking
place which decrease and increase the force respectively compared to a single cylinder which for
d/D = 1 seems to even cancel each other out. The gap-ratio of d/D = 0.5 is the case with the smallest
drag coefﬁcients.
The inertia coefﬁcients develops in very close vicinity of each other for the three different gap-ratios.
In general the gap-ratio of d/D = 2 is the one with the highest CM values, closely followed by the
d/D = 1 gap-ratio which lies almost on top. With only a bit smaller values is gap-ratio d/D = 0.5
placed. The overall tendency is that the 2x2 constellation yields lower CM values than for a single
cylinder, which was the same as observed for the 2x1 set up. It should be mentioned, even though the
lid is applied for this case the spacing between the cylinder and the holes in the lid have a tendency of
==PAGE:72==
63
CHAPTER 5. RESULTS - FORCE MEASUREMENTS
sucking air down which creates an air pocket. This have the appearance of a wave beneath the lid and
the effect is some what similar. This is especially present for the 2x2 constellation due the number
holes and cylinders. How much this have affected the results are unclear but it is deﬁnitely more than
for the previous cases. Non the less, the lid did have an effect as the next section will tell.

For the inertia coefﬁcients the development for both the case with and without the lid is almost con-
stant CM when gap-ratio is increasing. For the case with the lid the lines between the points are
very close to each other where a bit more space is found for the case without the lid. This was in
general also what was observed for both 1x2 and 2x1 constellations and is due to the surface wave
interaction.
The increase in gap-ratio does also give an increase in full structure blockage. Looking at Figure 5.35
the drag coefﬁcients tend to increase a bit for increased blockage and gap-ratio as one would expect.
Fort the inertia coefﬁcients the same is observed but the effect almost not visible. However, it is not
the effect of a wider overall structure but a consequence of a decrease in the shielding effect. If look-
ing at the 1x2 set up which isolates the effect of increase of the gap and overall blockage (see Figure
5.9 ) the tendency is somewhat opposite of the 2x2 set up. The effect of blockage for the overall struc-
ture for a porous structure, where the ﬂow have free pass through the gaps of the structure, does not
have the same effect which normally is expected when speaking about blockage.
To see how the 2x2 set up behaves relative to a single cylinder Figure 5.37 have been produced for the
case with the lid, where the force coefﬁcients have been normalised with their 1x1 counterpart.

Looking at the drag coefﬁcients the agreement with the numerical results and the experimental res-

the effect of changing the gap-ratio in the numerical model seems bigger than for the experimental
results. The numerical model predicts that all drag coefﬁcients for gap-ratio 1 and 2 should be above
the single cylinder value. This is not what is found by the experiments where only the biggest gap-
ratio is above the single cylinder values for all KC-numbers.
The inertia coefﬁcients shows even bigger disagreement between the numerical and experimental
results. All the numerical results are above the experimental results but the development of CM is
close to what the numerical model suggests with fairly close spaced CM values through out the KC-
range.

numerical results. The surface wave have a heavy inﬂuence on the CM values and a direct compare

The in-line force measurement in Figure 5.41a shows close to equally large forces for all four cylin-

four peaks for positive velocity and cylinder one and three peaks for negative velocity. This indicates
the same force phenomena as seen for the 2x1 set up where the shielding effect inﬂuence the force
development. The ensemble average will show this in greater detail.
For the lift forces in Figure 5.41b the lift forces show signs of working in opposite lift forces but the
forces for cylinder one and four seems much higher than for two and three. This indicates once more
that the aliment of the cylinder is not good enough to measure the relative small lift forces. The high
lift forces around one newton can very well be the effect of the lift force but it have been amplify by
an artiﬁcial force generated due to bad alignment.
The effect of the waves generated in general have been measured and presented in Appendix H. The
maximum waves have been recorded after a KC = 11.3 run .

5.8
Summary of the force measurements
A series of force measurements have been conducted on cylinder set ups consisting of a single cylin-
der, which mostly serves as a reference case, a 1x2 set up, a 2x1 set up which purpose is to illustrate
different effects of interaction. The last set up is a 2x2 constellation which serves as a simple large
scale porous structure.
The 1x1 set up is placed in the channel resulting in a blockage of γ = 0.143 which is relative low. This
is seen as almost no change in the force coefﬁcients is observed relative to the unconﬁned case. The
drag coefﬁcients follows very closely the same path as the unconﬁned case where the inertia coef-
ﬁcients show a little deviation from the unconﬁned case. This is concluded primarily to be caused
by the build up of a surface wave. As the cylinder is placed in a narrow channel the relative amount
==PAGE:80==
71
CHAPTER 5. RESULTS - FORCE MEASUREMENTS
of water moved together with the cylinder seems to increase. This reduces the value of the inertia
coefﬁcients as described in Section 4.2.4. For the drag coefﬁcient the surface wave tends to increase
the magnitude but the effect is not as clear. The overall conclusion for the single cylinder set up is,
that the blockage does not affect the force coefﬁcients signiﬁcantly.
For the 1x2 set up the blockage is now increased relative to the single cylinder. Additionally the gap
between the cylinder induces a jet ﬂow, especially for the gap-ratio of d/D = 0.5 and d/D = 1, which
increases the velocities on the cylinders. The gap-ration with the smallest gap-ratio does have the
biggest force coefﬁcients for both drag and inertia. These decreases as the gap is increased. The drag
coefﬁcient decrease almost evenly for each gap-ratio where as the inertia coefﬁcient increases the
most from d/D = 0.5 to d/D = 1 and only a slight change is observed when gap-ratio is increased to
d/D = 2.
The development of the force coefﬁcients relative to the full structure blockage shows that the force
coefﬁcients decrease when the full blockage increase. This is due to the gap-ratio increasing with the
blockage. It does there for not make sense to talk about the full structure blockage in a conventional
way for the 1x2 set up as the gap dominates the forces acting on the structure. Both the case with and
without the lid have the same tendency but as the surface wave the result with the lid on yields a bit
lower drag coefﬁcients and higher inertia coefﬁcients than the case without the lid.
The time series for the 1x2 set up did not show much conclusive as the lift forces are too small for the
experimental set up measure with high enough accuracy.
For the 2x1 set up the full structure blockage is constant for all gap-ratios as the gap increases in
the ﬂow direction. Shielding effects dominates this constellation and the forces measured are lower
than for the single cylinder with decrease in the force coefﬁcients with decrease in gap-ratio. The
smallest gap ratio yields the smallest force coefﬁcients which increase as the gap-ratio is increased.
The widest gap-ration generates drag and inertia coefﬁcients close to the values of a single cylinder.
The drag coefﬁcients is affected the most by shielding.
The time series of the 2x1 constellation shows that the cylinders have mean forces directed away
from each other for the smallest gap-ratio. This is linked to the low velocity in the gap which due to
the Bernoulli principal may have a larger pressure than a the undisturbed wake. This will reduce the
overall force acting on the up stream cylinder and at the same time push the cylinders away from
each other. The wake behind the down stream cylinder does also contribute actively to this effect
on the down stream cylinder. This effect seems to decrease with the increase of the cylinder. For the
d/D = 1 the effect is too small to say conclusive whether or not it acts any more but indications shows
it does. For d/D = 2 the effect is not to be seen any more.
For the 2x2 constellation the force develops in what appears to be a direct combination of the two
simple set ups with the shielding from 2x1 as the dominating feature. For the case with the lid, the
drag coefﬁcients for gap-ratio d/D = 0.5 are the lowest, below the results for the single cylinder. For
d/D = 1 the drag forces are almost the same as for a singe cylinder and for the d/D = 2 gap ratio the
drag forces are placed above the singe cylinder values. For the inertia coefﬁcients the development
with change in gap-ratio is very small. Almost constant inertia coefﬁcients are observed but they
tend to increase with a increase in gap-ratio.
The effect of the surface wave is relative signiﬁcant for this constellation as four cylinder moving in
phase make up for an effective wave generator. There for the results obtained for the case without
the lid is quite different than for the case with the lid. The development for change in gap-ratio is
the same, but the drag coefﬁcients are signiﬁcantly above what observed for the case with the lid on.
Opposite, the inertia coefﬁcients have dropped a great amount due to the surface wave.
The times series for the 2x2 constellation shows a similar picture of what is observed for the 2x1
constellation where shielding in the dominant feature of interaction. This is seen as the horizontal
cylinder pair have mean forces directed away from each other with the down stream cylinder experi-
encing the higher force during a period. This effects decrease with increasing gap-ratio. As the effect
of ﬂow speed also decrease with increasing gap-ratio, the total effect is that the force magnitude felt
==PAGE:81==
5.8. SUMMARY OF THE FORCE MEASUREMENTS
72
by the cylinder does not change that much as the effect of shielding is close to balanced by the in-
creased velocity ﬁeld for the cases investigated in this project.
The compare with the numerical model by Bjø rke (2015) shows overall that the numerical model
tends to have force coefﬁcients above the ﬁndings of the experiment with the lid on. The reason
for this is unclear but as the experiment is not fully submerged it may be that the numerical model
and the experiment do not relate directly. This is especially the case for the experiments with out
the lid, where the inertia coefﬁcients from the experiment is very much off relative to the numerical
model.
Lastly a comment should be linked to the effect of blockage for a large scale porous structure. Even
though the limited number of set ups investigated in this project makes it hard to say anything gen-
eral about large porous structures, it is found that the full structure blockage does not have the effect
which is normally linked to the blockage-phenomenon, as porous structure in this project have free
ﬂow though the center of the set up. This is further investigated in Section 5.9 where the full structure
forces are discussed.
==PAGE:82==
73
CHAPTER 5. RESULTS - FORCE MEASUREMENTS
5.9
Total Structure forces on a porous structure
The forces acting on a large scale porous structure is simply the sum of forces acting on the individual
cylinders. The predictability of the overall forces on a large scale porous structure is typically done
by a Morrison prediction with is the sum of the individual structural member. This may prove to
difﬁcult as the nature of a porous structure introduces interaction between the individual structural
members. Even for a simple porous structure like the 2x2 constellation investigated in this project, it
can be difﬁcult to come up with a simple correction term to take into account the porous nature. A
typical the application of large scaled porous structures are in moon pools, why also this ﬁeld have
developed correction expressions to take into account the blockage experienced in the moon pool
water when equipment is submerged in the pool.
The purpose of this section is to make a general compare between the measured total structure
forces, a simple free stream Morrison prediction where the free stream forces on each structural
member is used to determine the full structure force and the Morrison prediction using corrected
force coefﬁcients. The compare will use the full structure diameter and area as determining the full
structure force coefﬁcients.
The measured total force are found by summing the individual cylinder force measurement in the
time domain to create a time series, which represent the full structure loading. From the least square
method the drag and inertia coefﬁcients can be determined based on the Morrison approach, where
diameter and the area of the total structure changes with the gap-ratio. This means that also KC-
numbers will very with the gap-ratio.
The free stream approach is simply the free cylinder forces four times from which the force coef-
ﬁcients for the full structure can be determined. Also here the full structure diameter and area
changes.
The corrected force coefﬁcients will be found from the guide line concerning forces in offshore equip-
ment placed in moon pools, which is provided from Det Norske Veritas, (see Dnv (2011)). The cor-
rected drag- and inertia coefﬁcients are as follows
CD,cor =
1−0.5 Ab
A

1−Ab

(5.1)
CM,cor =
Ã

(5.2)
where Ab is the area of the structural elements in the moon pool, A is the area of the moon pool,
CD is the undisturbed drag coefﬁcient of the structure and CM is the undisturbed inertia coefﬁcient
of the structure. The two latter are the free stream values found for the total structure free stream
force coefﬁcients, at least for this case. Since the analysis is in 2D, the areas mentioned in the equa-
tions can be taken as the width of the actual width of the structural element facing the ﬂow (The
diameter of two cylinders) and the width of the channel, which acts like the a moon pool in terms of
conﬁnement.
Figure 5.45, 5.46 and 5.47 shows the drag- and inertia coefﬁcients for the measured total force, the
free stream total force and the corrected total force. Only the case of the 2x2 set up with the lid will be
used as the case without the lid is under heavy inﬂuence of the surface wave, which does that the free
stream values do not scale linearly. Also the corrections for CD and CM is for oscillating only with no
wave action as such. It should be noted that even tough the lid have been quite effective to suppress
the surface wave, the results obtained from the experiment may not simulate the ﬂow acting in a
moon pool to perfection.

==PAGE:84==
75
CHAPTER 5. RESULTS - FORCE MEASUREMENTS
To start with, the drag force tendency is that the measured full structure drag forces decrease with
increase in the gap-ratio which is the opposite of what was observed when the cylinders were ana-
lysed individually in Section 5.6. As the full structure diameter increase with the gap ratio, the drag
coefﬁcient becomes proportionally smaller which is the effect of porosity. The bigger space between
the cylinders, the higher the porosity.
Comparing the measured total drag coefﬁcients to the free stream Morrison predictions, the overall
tendency is that not much deviation is found. The tendency follows what also is observed in Figure
5.33 where the forces are below that of a single cylinder for d/D = 0.5, approximate the same for
d/D = 1 and above the single cylinder values for d/D = 2.
Compared to the corrected drag coefﬁcients, the measured values are far below. The corrections do
take in to account the blockage from the cylinders but as shielding is quite dominant an overestima-
tion would be expected. For d/D = 0.5 the greatest over estimation by the corrected drag coefﬁcient
is found. This is also the case where shielding is most dominant. As the effect of shielding decreases
for d/D = 1 the overestimation is not as big. The same goes for d/D = 2 where the difference is the
smallest but still signiﬁcant.
For the measured inertia coefﬁcients an increase is observed for decreasing gap-ratio, thus the op-
posite of the drag coefﬁcient and the same as observed for the single cylinders in Section 5.6. The
total structure area decrease with decreasing gap-ratio (and porosity) making the inertia coefﬁcient
to increase relative, as the inertia coefﬁcients acting on the individual cylinder is close to constant for
changes in gap-ratio ( see Figure 5.33).
If comparing the measured inertia coefﬁcients to the total structure inertia coefﬁcients from free
stream prediction they follow each other closely, but the free steam values are slightly above the
measured. Again this can also be found in the analysis of the 2x2 individual cylinder force coefﬁ-
cients, as presented in Figure 5.33 which also shows the inertia coefﬁcients are all very closely placed
just below the value of the single cylinder. This tendency is mirrored to the total structure forces.
Looking at the corrected inertia coefﬁcients, they too are above the measured inertia coefﬁcients.
Again the shielding effect may be the cause to this, but the difference is not as big as for the drag
coefﬁcient. The tendency is still that the smallest gap-ratio yields the biggest difference between the
corrected force coefﬁcients and the experimental force coefﬁcients.
To summarise, the total structure forces for the investigated case of 2x2 cylinders shows that shielding
is a governing parameter for predicting the total forces on a large scaled porous structure, which is not
taken into account by the applied correction procedure. When placed in a conﬁned space the ﬂow
speed up is relatively well balanced by the shielding effect, especially for the inertia coefﬁcients which
shows high agreement with the prediction for a free cylinder. It should be noted this is exclusive for
the geometry in the 2x2 set up. It may not be the general case as different distances to structural
members may create different shielding and ﬂow speed up effects.
==PAGE:85==
6
Results - ﬂow visualisation
Along with the force measurement a ﬂow visualisation have been made for KC = 10.05 with peak
free stream velocity of Um = 0.134 m/s. The goal is to be able to explain some of the forces and the
development of these across the different set-ups using the PIV-analysis. For each set up a number of
cross sections have been chosen to produce velocity proﬁles from to have a clear look at the velocity
distribution. For a more general perspective velocity maps have been produced to visualise the ﬂow
structures. All PIV results are presented as an ensemble average of eight periods in order to make
a more robust velocity prediction. The PIV analysis have been performed as described in the PIV-
validation (Section 4.2.5). Different PIV analysis with different frame rate have been used, but this
will clearly be pointed out. The ﬂow visualisations for each constellation have been divided into
two sections for each gap-ratio. One for the velocity proﬁles in interesting cross sections and one
for the velocity maps showing the overall ﬂow development. A summarising overview of the ﬂow
visualisation is to ﬁnd in Section 6.4 lastly in this main section.
6.1
Flow visualisation for 1x2
The purpose of the ﬂow visualisation for the 1x2 constellation is primarily to investigate the impact
of the presents of the two cylinders. Both to see how the velocities distribute but also in the hope to
quantify the increased ﬂow velocities.
Figure 6.1 shows a velocity map with lines marking the cross sections used for velocity proﬁles. The
cylinders are marked with rectangles. Remember that due to the perspective changing as the cylinder
move, the masked area in the PIV analysis ends up as rectangles.
This ﬁgure is the reference for all the velocity proﬁles extracted for this constellation. The spacing
bewteen the cylinders will vary, but the horizontal position of the cylinders will not change as such.
Perspective distortion may make the cylinders appear in different sizes, but this is one of the artefacts
which occurs when the set up moves.

velocity around the cylinder negative.

Figure 6.2: Velocity proﬁles for the 1x2 set up with gap-ratio d/D = 0.5 in the same cross section as the cylin-

The velocity proﬁles in Figure 6.2 shows an unexpected picture of the ﬂow velocity in the gap. For
ωt = 42 the velocity suddenly drops to zero for then to increase to just 0.05 m/s. This seems to be
==PAGE:87==
6.1. FLOW VISUALISATION FOR 1X2
78
too small velocities compared to the expectations that the ﬂow would speed up. The reason for this
is a combination of two things: Firstly the ﬂow between the cylinders are higher than the overall ﬂow
velocity, thus it may be too high for the 30 fps to resolve it correctly as the particle displacement be-
comes too big. Secondly it is a very narrow passage compared to the overall ﬂow ﬁeld recorded. The
number of detected particles in the gap is simply not high enough to give resolve the ﬂow properly
for the 30 fps. analysis which uses the 64 × 64 px window. It is difﬁcult to do something about the
resolution of the images as it require higher resolution on the camera or a camera position which is
stationary relative to the set up which makes it possible to get closer to the gap.
A path to partly solve the issue of the lack of resolution in the gap is to "upgrade" the analysis to a 60
fps analysis to give a better picture of the ﬂow in the gap, as the window can be smaller. Also a proﬁle
just down stream the gap can be visualised. The velocity just outside the gap should be very close to
the velocity inside the gap.
A 60 fps. analysis with a smaller interrogation area is used to try to resolve the velocities in the gap
better. The interrogation area is down scaled a bit to 40x40 pixels to better be able to visualise the gap
velocity. It comes with a prize of decrease in overall ﬂow velocity accuracy for the ﬂow as seen in the
validation section 4.2.5. The cross section through the cylinder is seen in Figure 6.3.

The vortex formation from the two cylinders creates symmetric vortices from the two cylinder sides
which are facing away from each other. The vorticity from the two vortices are rotating in opposite
directions which creates a ﬂow towards the gap. It does not seem to feed that much water in to the
gap as the gradient arrows slightly indicate that they curl op rather than inject water to into the gap.
A pair of vortices can also be seen to the left in the ﬁgure. These are the consequence of the jet ﬂow
from the gap which shoot out. The vorticity is opposite than for the vortices developed in the cylinder
boundary layer. An additional vortex is formed at the bottom cylinder bottom right corner. The other
corners may also have small vortices but any slight miss alignment can make one of them to grow to
a level where the PIV analysis detects more clearly.
The effects of blockage can be seen in the area around the cylinder where the water still have some
velocity left as the outer ﬂow have not slowed down the water just yet. The same was observed for
Figure 6.6.
The ﬂow develops to the maximum free stream velociry which can be seen in Figure 6.8. The before
mentioned issues can be seen again, as almost no velocity in between the cylinders are detected.
The shaded area behind the cylinders does also show signs that the PIV analysis lags correlation for
the interrogation areas as the shaddows from the cylinders makes a drop in light intensity. However,
the effect of blockage can clearly be seen as the ﬂow velocity is increased at the sides of the cylin-
ders.

different things:

gap-velocity with much better than for the two previous gap-ratios. This is expected to be the bigger
gap which allows more particles to pass through, thus more particles for the PIV analysis to work with.
The velocity in the gap is roughly the same as the velocities on the out side of the cylinders.

6.2
Flow visualisation for 2x1
The ﬂow visualisation for the 2x1 set up is to see how the ﬂow development takes place in the gap
between the two cylinders. The shielding effect have shown to be present in the measrements of the
forces as described in Section 5.4. A problem will soon become clear for the smallest gap-ratio as the
resolution of the equipment is not good enough to visualise the ﬂow in between the cylinder very
well. The results will be presented and the uncertainties will be elaborated upon through out the
section. As before both 30 fps. analysis and 60 fps. analysis will be used and clearly stated which one
is used.
Figure 6.26 below shows the cross sections which are used to produce velocity proﬁles for this ﬂow
visualisation. The a single period is representing an ensemble average of eight periods which starts
with ﬂow to the right.

original footage is inspected a rather chaotic particle movement is found. This makes it hard for the
PIV-analysis to predict the velocities. Especially for an area that small compared to the overall ﬂow
area and camera resolution.

sign of disturbance as a small peak shoot out from the rest of the proﬁle. This is found to be the jet of
increased velocity which is dragged in behind the cylinders as Figure 6.33 will show later on.

ﬂow development is relative robust as the scatter of the velocity predictions is relative low.

disturb the full development of the vortices. Other wise the ﬂow pattern is very similar to the case of
d/D = 0.5 which is described in Section 6.2.2.

higher resulting in higher forces as found in Section 5.4.1. The velocities are up to the order of 0.1
m/s which is around 75% of free stream velocity.

creased relative to the case of d/D = 1 in Figure 6.40, which is expected to be due to the bigger vortex
formation in the gap.

6.4
Summery of the ﬂow visualisation
The ﬂow visualisation for both 1x2, 2x1 and 2x2 constellations all have the same overall issues of
resolving the velocity between the cylinders. First thing that the set up moves relative to the camera
position which makes it limited how close the recording can take place, as the structure eventually
will move out of the frame if the is placed to close. Secondly the ﬂow is oscillating meaning that a
wide range of velocities acts. This makes it difﬁcult to chose the the settings for the PIV as the particle
displacement is a governing aspect of choosing the right interrogation areas and frame rate. Despise
these challenges some general tendencies for all constellations have become clear as summarised in
the following.
==PAGE:125==
6.4. SUMMERY OF THE FLOW VISUALISATION
116
For the 1x2 constellation the overall dominating ﬂow pattern for gap-ratio d/D = 0.5 and d/D = 1
is that a pair of vortices form on the outside of both cylinders which curl up behind the cylinder
at ﬂow reversal. A jet ﬂow is generated in the gap of the cylinder which shoots out into the wake.
This induced a pair of vortices in the wake with opposite vorticity than the vortices formed at the
cylinders. At times a small vortex can form at the upstream corners of both cylinders but it is quite
small relative to the other vortex action.
The gap-velocities for d/D = 0.5 was found to be around 0.2 to 0.25 m/s which is an 68% average
increase over the free stream velocity. From the velocity proﬁles it seems that the velocities close to
the cylinders in general are as high. Almost the exact same velocity tendencies was found for d/D = 1.
From the ﬂow visualisation, it seems fair to say that the increased velocities for gap-ratio 0.5 and 1 is
not that different, which may be due to the narrow gap which makes the ﬂow seek around the two
cylinders. This is further discussed at Figure 7.1.
For d/D = 2 a change in the gap-ﬂow is observed as the jet-like ﬂow is too weak for the vortices to
form down stream. The cylinders do both form a pair of symmetric vortices in stead. The increased
velocities are not as high as the ones for the smaller gap-ratios - only around 42%. These values have
to be mentioned with caution, as the exact velocity predictions of the PIV in the gaps can be a bit
uncertain.
The 2x1 constellation the show an outer ﬂow development very similar for all gap-ratios investigated.
From the down steam cylinder a pair of vortices are formed. A small vortex have also been seen form
over the up stream cylinder corners but it is small compared to the down stream vortices.
For the smallest gap-ratio the velocity in the gap is quite low compared to the overall ﬂow velo-
city. The PIV analysis suggests almost zero velocity but as turbulence makes the particles move in
a chaotic way, together with a small space, does that the PIV simply cannot resolve the exact velocit-
ies. However, it may be concluded that the velocity in the gap is indeed very low. Also it is noticed
that the small gap does not allow the development of vortices as the space between the cylinders is
too tight. For the wider gaps the vortex formation is possible in the gap and this starts to increase the
ﬂow velocity. Due to the vortex formation the ﬂow can be directed opposite of the free stream ﬂow
for both the widest gap-ratios.The general velocity for the d/D = 1 gap-ratio is up to 0.06 m/s where
d/D = 2 can have velocities of d/D = 0.1 m/s according to the PIV. This is respectively 45% and 75%
of the free stream velocity.
Lastly the 2x2 constellation shows a ﬂow pattern which is a combination of the 1x2 and the 2x1 set
up. The increased velocity in the horizontal gap was adopted from the 1x2 set up, apparently with no
change in the velocities as such. As the gap increase from d/D = 0.5 to d/D = 1 the velocity seems
unchanged in the horizontal gap. For the widest gap-ration of d/D = 2 a drop in the gap velocity
is observed as the jet-like ﬂow do not develops as such. The gap velocities are close to the natural
increased ﬂow ﬁeld which occurs due to the presents of a cylinder.
The for the gap between the horizontal cylinder pairs, the tendency follows the 2x1 set up as well,
where shielding reduces the overall ﬂow velocity. In the gap between the horizontal cylinder pair, the
velocity for the smallest gap-ratio of d/D = 0.5 seems to have almost zero velocity. From the time
series in the center points of the gaps, all the velocity predictions are scattered closely around zero
leaving no exact velocity other than what appears to be a mean velocity of zero. For wider gap-ratios
the vortex formation starts to appear which introduces velocities in the gap moving opposite of the
free stream, just like for the 2x1.
It seems that the 2x2 constellation adopts more or less directly the ﬂow development from both the
1x2 and the 2x1 constellations. This was also observed for the force measurements. The shielding
effect and the increased jet-like ﬂow from the horizontal gap coexists without signs of interference as
such.
==PAGE:126==
7
Discussion and linking of the forces and
the PIV results
7.1
Linking the PIV results and the force measurements
The purpose of doing both force measurements and ﬂow visualisation is to make a quantitative and
qualitative connection between the measured forces and the velocity ﬁeld of the given set up. Only
the cases where the lid have been applied will be treated in relation to the PIV as the PIV does not say
anything about the effect of the surface wave. Also the PIV is only made for KC = 10.05. There for only
force measurements for KC = 11.3 will be used in the direct compare as this is the closest KC-value.
It is expected that the relative tendency will somewhat apply to lower KC-values as well.
7.1.1
The 1x2 set up
The force measurements for the 1x2 set up is increasing for decreasing gap-ratio as seen in Section
5.2. The force development for KC = 11.3 is seen in Table 7.1. Clearly the drag coefﬁcients are
most affected by the combination of two side-by-side cylinders. The inertia coefﬁcient are not as
much.
Table 7.1: Drag and inertia foces for KC = 11.3. The bottom line is the velocity in the gap also at KC = 10.05.

The cause to the force development can be found in the velocity around the cylinder where a clear
velocity increase is observed. According to the times series of the gap velocity for the d/D = 0.5 gap
ratio the average peak velocity for the full period is 0.225 m/s. The same average peak velocity was
found for the gap-ratio of d/D = 1. This is a bit unexpected as the gap is doubled from d/D = 0.5 to
d/D = 1. As mentioned before this can be due to the PIV not being able to detect the exact velocity in
the smallest gap. An alternative explanation could be that the small gap ratio is too small for the ﬂow
velocity to increase further but instead the water is directed around the cylinders. This could explain
why the inertia coefﬁcient experience the highest increase when the gap is narrowed from d/D = 1
to d/D = 0.5 as this would increase the effective area of which the inertia of the water can act. To
investigate this a zoom in on the vector plots is made as seen in Figure 7.1.

It seems that the ﬂow for the gap-ratio of d/D = 0.5 have a tendency to be directed around both
the cylinder more than for d/D = 1 where the ﬂow is directed more straight on each cylinder which
makes the water intake for the gap higher.
However, it should be underlined that the PIV analysis presented in this report may not be accurate
enough to conclude the actual gap-velocities for the smallest gap. A more precise investigation must
be made to conﬁrm the real exact gap velocity, either by use of PIV, LVDT or hot wire anemometry.
Alternatively a CFD analysis should also be able to conﬁrm or reject this.
For the gap-ratio of d/D = 2 a clear drop in the gap velocity is seen as expected. The distance between
the cylinder have increased to a level where the interaction may not be the cause to the increased
forces relative to the single cylinder. Increased the blockage by the presents of two cylinders is caus-
ing the overall ﬂow to speed up. This is supported by the ﬁndings of Chern et al. (2010) who made a
numerical investigation for case of side-by-side cylinder, where interaction is almost not taking place
for d/D = 2.

as seen in Table 7.2. Again only KC = 11.3 is compared to the PIV as is only have been made for
KC = 10.05.

The gap velocity is in general very low which cause the pressure to increase due to the Bernoulli
principal. How much the pressure change is difﬁcult to predict from the velocities alone. However,
the effect have been observed for d/D = 0.5 by the upstream cylinder having reduced over all forces.
The down stream cylinder exceed the force for the upstream cylinder as described in Section 5.5.1
indicating that a pressure acts.

The result from the product of the 1x2 and 2x1 normalised drag and inertia coefﬁcients are very close
to that is measured for the 2x2 set up. Especially the drag coefﬁcient show good agreement. The fact
that the forces from the 1x2 and 2x1 set ups can be directly combined to give the forces for the 2x2 set
up is further conﬁrmed by the ﬂow visualisation which both qualitatively and quantitatively shows
a direct combination of the ﬂow patterns and velocity development. The 2x2 makes up a simple
large scaled porous structure of a symmetric kind which may be the cause of this connection. If the
structure have been asymmetric or consisted of more cylinder it may not have been the case.
==PAGE:129==
7.2. THE EFFECT OF POROSITY
120
This linear scaling of the set ups may also be seen for the other KC-values investigated in the pro-
ject as seen in Appendix F where the deviations show no more than 9% deviations with the highest
deviations in the low KC-values where the uncertainties are largest.
7.2
The effect of porosity
The only structure in this investigation which does have the characteristics of a true porous structure
is the 2x2 set up. A large scaled porous structure needs to be consisting at leas a few structural ele-
ment in order to be a porous structure so in that sense the four cylinder set up is close to the most
simple porous structure one could have. This clearly have the effect that each element contribute
by a great amount to the overall force and ﬂow development. If a more complex structure is con-
sidered the individual structural element becomes of less and less importance to the overall force.
The straight forward connection between the two simpler cases of 1x2 and 2x1 constellations where
the enhancing effect from the blockage and jet ﬂow are balanced by the shielding effect in a close
to linear way, clearly shows that the effect of porosity is governed by these three parameters. How-
ever, it is difﬁcult to foresee the direct relation if not a speciﬁc investigation of the exact structure
is made and it will most certainly not be the case that more complex structures behave as the 2x2
constellation investigated in this project.
In Section 5.9 both a full structure prediction is made based upon free stream values and corrected
values according to the guide line from Det Norske Veritas. The full width and area of the structure
was used for the predictions. It was found that the free stream values was all closely located around
the measured full structure values and the corrected values all overestimates the forces. Especially
the corrected drag coefﬁcients are above the measured. The corrected values are based upon the free
stream value which uses the full diameter and area of the structure, regardless of the porosity.
The fact that the free stream values are closer to predict the total force on the structure does not say
much about a general porous structure, as this speciﬁc 2x2 structure yields drag and inertia coefﬁ-
cients for the individual cylinder which are close to the values of a single free cylinder. If the structure
does not have equal sized gap-width in x- and y-direction it will most likely not be the case that a free
stream value prediction will predict this close to the measured full structure values. The time frame
of this project did unfortunately not allow for further investigations of more advanced set ups with
different gap-ratios for x- and y-directions as too much time went with making the experimental set
up work at all. This could be a topic for further research.
==PAGE:130==
8
Conclusion
The goal for this project have been to contribute to the general knowledge of large scale porous struc-
tures by experimental analysis by determining the ﬂow forces and link these to a ﬂow visualisation.
The porous structure was chosen to consist of generic square-proﬁle cylinders of 40×40 mm which
could be arranged in different constellations depending on the ﬁndings, so different aspects could
be investigated. The time frame allowed only for the most basic structures consisting of a side-by-
side (1x2) arrangement, a tandem (2x1) arrangement and the combined case of four cylinders placed
in a square conﬁguration (2x2) which is the only real porous structure. All three set ups have been
oscillated in a 280 mm. channel with KC-values from 1.1 to 11.3, relative to the single cylinder, with
the period of T = 1 second. The square cylinder have stationary separation points regardless of the
Reynolds number, thus the period of one second was chosen to produce a high force signal on the
load cells. The gap-ratio between cylinder varies between 0.5, 1 and 2 to investigate the different in-
teraction effect for different distances between the cylinders. For all set ups the cylinders have been
attached on load cells capable of measuring the in-line force and the lift force. Unfortunately the
measurement of the lift force was not of the quality expected, which made them unreliable. Further
more ﬂow visualisation for all set ups have been performed for KC = 10.05. PIV was used to this. The
PIV set up have not been applied with the purpose of highly exact velocity measurements but more
as a ﬂow visualisation with quantitative results.
For the 1x2 set up it was found that the constellation of the two cylinders increase the near by ﬂow
velocities, relative to a single cylinder, due to two aspects. The ﬁrst is simply the presents of two
cylinders in a channel which causes blockage to increase. Also the gap between the cylinders creates
a jet-ﬂow which have high velocity, especially for the two smallest gap-ratios. For the smallest gap-
ratio it was found that the gap is too small for the jet-ﬂow to develop fast enough which forces the
water around the two cylinders. This makes the two single cylinders to approach the effect of a single
block. In general the forces have proven to increase for decreasing gap-ratio.
For the 2x1 set up the dominant phenomenon is shielding. The upstream cylinder provides shielding
for the down stream cylinder. The effect of this is a bit counter intuitive as the down stream cylin-
der experiences the larger forces. The shielding creates a region of slow moving water between the
cylinder which may have larger pressure than the outer ﬂow due to the Bernoulli principal. For the
smallest gap-ratios the water does not have a direction as such but for the higher gap ratios the form-
ation of vortices makes the ﬂow to ﬂow against the main ﬂow last in the period but still with smaller
overall velocity than the outer ﬂow. The shielding effect decreases with increasing gap-ratio making
the overall forces on the cylinder to increase with gap-ratio and the two cylinder mean forces ap-
proach each other in magnitude. The effect of interaction is almost gone for the widest gap-ratio of
d/D = 2
For the 2x2 constellation the force and ﬂow pattern is a combination of the two previous set ups.
The increased ﬂow due to blockage and jet-ﬂow seems to be balanced by the shielding effect both in
terms of force magnitudes, which makes the forces for the individual cylinder in the 2x2 set up to be
relative close to the single cylinder value, and in terms of the ﬂow pattern development. The direct
link between the constellations becomes apparent when looking at the force coefﬁcients normalised
121
==PAGE:131==
122
by the single cylinder values. It turns out that the product of the normalised force coefﬁcients for the
1x2 and 2x1 set up yields the normalised force coefﬁcients for the 2x2 set up with only up to 10 % de-
viations for the smallest KC-values. Also the effect of shielding between the horizontal cylinder pairs
have the same characteristics found for the 2x1 set up where the down stream cylinder experiences
the highest forces, especially for the small gap-ratios.
The total structure forces and ﬂow development for a large scale porous structure is governed by
shielding, blockage and jet-ﬂow which for a very simple structure, like the 2x2 set up, may have a re-
lative simple connection. However, the complexity of the structure should not increase much before
this simple connection may become much more complex and unpredictable, which leads on to the
possibilities of further investigations
Suggestions for further research on the topic of large scale porous struc-
tures
As too much time, unfortunately, was spend on making the experimental set up work as intended,
a number of more complex set ups could have been relevant to investigate. How the apparent dir-
ect connection between the cases apply for set ups with varying gap-ratios in horizontal and vertical
spacings is a direct follow up on this project, which would be interesting to investigate further. This
can also be expanded to circular cylinders to see how interaction behaves for these. It is suggested
that a better carriage system and displacement control is used, if the measurements should be of
higher quality than those found in this project.
In connection with further investigations, it should be mentioned that a fully submerged experi-
mental set up would be an advantage as the surface wave issue will not be a problem. For the case
of better investigation by PIV it will also be advantageous to have a stationary set up relative to the
camera. This would make it possible to get closer to the cylinder to make a highly detailed analysis
of the ﬂow and turbulence development.
It could also be relevant to study more complex structures consisting of i.e 5-10 members. This could
be done by mounting the individual member on a common set up mount from which the full force is
measured. This could be used to test and develop the prediction of the full structure force for a more
complex large scale porous structure.
For the sake of replicating the numerical results found from Bjø rke (2015) a fully submerged set up
will also be necessary. Otherwise a new investigation of the performance of a numerical model can
be veriﬁed by a series of experiments similar to these. The main difference would then be that the
experiments should be executed before the numerical model is made as this will be easier than the
other way around.

==PAGE:137==
B. LOAD CELL CALIBRATION
128
B
Load Cell Calibration
The load cells used for the experiments is the TAL201 from HT-sensor Technology Co., Ldt. This is
chosen since it is a parallel bridge lay out, making it capable of measuring the section force rather
than a bending moment, as shown in this Section. This is advantageous, since the load can be found
directly in stead of doing a calculation based on the moment.
Two load cells are used in order to measure the in-line force and lift force on a single cylinder in the
validation experiment. Initially one load cell is tested to conﬁrm that it behaves as it should. When is
is conﬁrmed, the second load cell is calibrated on the assumption that it behaves similar to the ﬁrst
one, i.e. measuring the section force. The two load cells used for the validation experiment form the
example of how the rest load cells have been calibrated.
The volt signal from the load cells are ampliﬁes 1000 times in order to make the signal detectable for
the equipment.
Initial calibration
The ﬁrst calibration of a single load cell is done by applying load directly at the tip of the load cell, as
it was intended for.
The volt output for a given load, is measured for 20s. The average of the volt output form the connec-
tion between the given load and the expected volt output. The load cell will be affected by loads in
both directions, thus it is important to verify that the response to load is consistent independent of
the load direction.
Also the weight of the load cell have to be corrected for. This is done by doing a measurement of the
volt output for no load in both directions of the load cell. The difference in voltage corresponds to
the double weight of the load cell, which affects the output. By knowing the relation between load
change and volt change, it is possible to determine the load cell weight to be corrected for. The self
weight of the load cell, which affects the strain gauges is 89.3g
In Figure B.2 the load cell response is plotted, where the weight of the load cell is taken into account.
The load range is chosen beyond what is needed for the experiment, simply to see if the behaviour is
linear for high loads as well.

The response is perfectly linear. The calibration line is given as
V = 0.00133·Load +0.1568
(B.3)
The intersection with the y-axis is not that interesting at this point, since different equipment at-
tached to the load cell will shift this zero-point. The slope of the line, however, is important since it
should be the same no matter how the load is applied.
Calibration of Load Cell 1 and Load Cell 2 with long arm
To investigate the load cell response when an arm is attached to the load cell. The weight of the arm
is also affecting the load cell and needs to be added to the applied weight. The weight of the arm inc.
bolts is 128.76g.
The calibration is done for the same load cell as in the initial calibration. Because the increased
moment from the arm yields a higher strain in the load cell, level of strain gauge deformation will be
higher on the individual strain gauge. There for, in order to protect the strain gauges, a lower range
of loads have been applied. The load range is still beyond the loads expected to be recorded, thus the
calibration is valid. The calibration can be seen in Figure B.3a and the values for the plot can be seen
in Tabel B.2.

Again the response is perfectly linear. The calibration line is given as
V = 0.00133·Load +0.1325
(B.4)
The zero-value (intersection with the y-axis) is not the same as for the initial calibration, but this was
expected, since the arm acts on the load cell now. The slope of the line is exactly the same as before,
conﬁrming that the load cells are not affected by the length of the arm of which the load is applied,
thus it can be concluded that the load cells responds to the section forces.

but is no issue as long as the loads cells are numbered and treated accordingly.

C. EFFECT OF HAMMING FILTERING AND EIGENFREQUENCY OF CYLINDER.
132
C
Effect of Hamming ﬁltering and Eigenfrequency of cylinder.
This Appendix servers the purpose of showing the effect of the Hamming ﬁlter applied in order to
remove noise from the data ﬁles. The ﬁlter have been set to remove frequencies above 3 Hz. The
experiment is executed at 1 Hz, which should mean that the ﬁltered signal contains all the relevant
information about the force time series.
In order to verify that the eigenfrequency of the set up is far away from the 1 Hz region where the
measurements will take place, an excitation of the cylinder and the measurement of the signal have
been made. The unﬁltered spectra for this is seen in Figure C.4.
0
10
20
30
40
50
60
0
0.5
1
1.5x 10
−3
Frequency [Hz]
Spcetral density
Figure C.4: Spectra for the eigenfrequency of the cylinder. The peak is at 18.1 Hz.
The eigenfrequency of far away from the experiment periods, so the interference of vortex induced
vibrations are not likely. The effect of the water will act as a damper. This will lower the eigenfre-
quency of the cylinder, but as seen in the apparent frequency interference is still relative far away
from the frequency of 1 Hz.
The ﬁltered and unﬁltered spectra analysis for the single cylinder set up will show how the frequen-
cies are placed relative to the 1Hz area of interest. Figure C.6, C.7 and C.8 shows the ﬁltered and
unﬁltered spectra for all KC-values. It is seen that for all cases the ﬁltered spectra have full amplitude
at 1 Hz where as all other frequencies have been removed from the spectra. A zoom-in have been
made for KC = 4.5 to see this more clearly in Figure C.5

==PAGE:155==
G. VERIFICATION OF SURFACE WAVE INDEPENDENCE OF PERIOD
146
G
Veriﬁcation of surface wave independence of period
The purpose of this appendix is to verify that the effect of the surface wave cannot be reduced signi-
ﬁcantly more by reducing the period. The idea was the even tough the lid did suppress the surface
wave it did still cause some disturbances with respect to measure the undisturbed force coefﬁcients.
The assumption was that the effect of the wave scaled close to linear for the velocities used in the
overall investigation. This is seen in the 1x1 - single cylinder case with out the lid, where all three
periods of 1, 1½ and 2 seconds all yielded close to the same result.
An experiment was performed where the period was reduced to 1.5 for KC = 8.6 to verify the assump-
tion of the period not having signiﬁcant effect on the results. Figure G.10 shows with red the result in
relation to the other results with the lid on for a 1x2 constellation. The gap-ratio of d/D = 1 was used
as the veriﬁcation set up.

4
6
8

12

Figure G.10: CD and CM The not ﬁlled markers are cylinder 1 and the ﬁlled ones are cylinder 2. The red point
show the veriﬁcation experiment for T = 1.5 at KC = 8.6
As expected the result is very much in agreement with the general tendency and no more investiga-
tions have been conducted.
==PAGE:156==
147
H
Effect of wave generation
This Appendix is to clarify the effect of the generated waves due to the set up movement. The cyl-
inders moving back and fourth is effectively working as a wave generator. The wave loads on the
cylinders is measured to quantify the amount of error the waves potentially cause.
1x2 set up
Figure H.11 below shows the measured wave loads just after the experiment have been brought to

==PAGE:159==
I. THE VALIDATION ISSUE, AND THE SOLUTION
150
I
The Validation issue, and the solution
This Appendix is the result of approximate a month and a half of trouble shooting in order to make
the validation experiment work as expected. The results from the validation experiments showed
not only a deviation from previous studies, but also a tendency of randomness in how the results
behaved from experiment to experiment as described trough out this appendix.
The carriage is controlled by a hydraulic piston as described in Section 3.1.2. To conﬁrm the be-
haviour of the piston relative to the volt input, a calibration was made. It was soon found that the
hydraulics had a problem in terms of a jump when the piston changed direction at each half cycle,
which was believed to causing the trouble with the validation. The piston went to service in the
middle of the project, in the pursuit of ﬁxing the un-smooth run issue, but it was not problems with
the hydraulics it self, which causes the jump. Further investigation eventually found that the excit-
ation output from the control box causes the problem. The box is an old wave generator designed
at DTU back in 1983, and is designed so that it can adjust the position of the piston according to a
feedback from a LVDT mounted on the side of the hydraulic cylinder. It is believed that this causes
the problem, but no replacement control box was available during the project.
When the LVDT was investigated, it was discovered that the LVDT also have a phase lag of about
1/30 sec. when logged on the computer. This causes in wrong derivations of Cd and CM as the
displacement, velocity and acceleration does not correspond to the force measured. To produce the
correct displacement measurements, an external draw wire encoder was installed to the set-up. Only
the validation experiment is affected by this, as all the experiment afterwards is produced after the
hydraulics went to service. The issue of the un-smooth cylinder moving can be ﬁltered away, so only
the signal from the oscillations is used for analysis.
After the repair and ﬁx of the issue it was experienced that the hydraulic has the habit of performing
badly if running for too long time. This means that the un-smooth run of the hydraulics becomes
worse as oil temperature rises. This made the generation of results a longer process than anticipated,
why the number of experiments is a bit less than hoped for at the beginning of the project.
